Four Characteristics of Intellectual Forms
Enumerates four characteristics of intellectual forms distinct from material forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four key characteristics that distinguish intellectual forms from material forms according to the philosophers.

Core Idea
Intellectual forms defy the limits of material space, strength, and permanence.

The philosophers identify four unique traits of intelligible forms that set them apart from physical forms. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for grasping how the soul knows and how ideas persist beyond matter. Let's explore each one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the enumeration of four characteristics and the two additional differences at the end.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the four characteristics
Locate the four distinct points: (1) variation in inherence, (2) large forms inhering with small, (3) weak not effaced by strong, (4) no necessary removal and easy retrieval.
Each is introduced with 'ثانياً', 'ثالثاً', etc.
3
Contrast with material forms
For each characteristic, note how the passage explicitly contrasts with material forms. This contrast is the main teaching tool.
Material forms are given as the opposite in each case.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: Why do these distinctions matter for how we understand knowledge and the soul? The commentary will help.
The soul's immateriality is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني - المقصد السادس : قول الحكماء في الصور العقلية
الشكل الأول، وكذا المادة المتصورة بالصورة النارية يستحيل أن تتصور معها بصورة أخرى (بل) الصور العقلية (متفاوتة) في الحلول، فإن النفس إذا كانت خالية عن العلوم كان تصورها لشيء من الحقائق عسيراً جداً، وإذا اتصفت ببعض العلوم زاد استعدادها للباقي وسهل انتقاشها به . ( الثاني : تحل الكبيرة ) من الصور العقلية (في محل الصغيرة ) منها معا ولذلك تقدر النفس على تخيل السموات والأرض والجبال والأمور الصغيرة بالمرة معاً، بخلاف الصور المادية فإن العظيمة منها لا تحل في محل الصغيرة مجتمعة معها ( الثالث : لا ينمحي الضعيف بالقوي) يعني أنَّ الصورة العقلية للكيفية الضعيفة لا تزول عن القوة المدركة بسبب حصول صورة الكيفية القوية فيها بخلاف الخارجية، فإنَّ الكيفية الضعيفة منها تنمحي عن المادة عند حصول الكيفية القوية فيها ( الرابع : ( الصورة العقلية إذا حصلت في العاقلة ( لا يجب زوالها وإذا زالت سهل استرجاعها من غير حاجة إلى تجشم كسب جدید، بخلاف الصور الخارجية فإنَّها واجبة الزوال عن المادة العنصرية لاستحالة بقاء قواها أبداً وإذا زالت احتيج في استرجاعها إلى مثل السبب الأول، ومن الفرق بينهما أنَّ الصور الخارجية قد تكون محسوسة بالحواس الظاهرة بخلاف الصور العقلية، ومنها أنَّ الصور العقلية كلية بخلاف الخارجية
قوله : ( تحل (الكبيرة أي الصورة الحالة المقدارية . قوله : ( ولذا تقدر النفس إلخ هذا مبني على أن صور الجزئيات الجسمانية حاصلة في النفس ولو بتوسط الحواس على ما هو التحقيق نص عليه الشارح قدس سره في حواشي المطالع. قوله: (مجتمعة معها) وأما على التعاقب فجائز بناء على أن الهيولى لا مقدار لها فى نفسها. قوله : (سهل) أي في بعض الأوقات . قوله : (غير متمانعة والسر فيه أن التقابل والتماثل ونظائرهما في المنع من الاجتماع من أحكام الوجود الأصيلي لا الظلي كما سبق . قوله: (وكذلك المادة (المتصورة أشار بإيراد المثالين إلى أنَّ المراد بالصورة الخارجية هاهنا أعم من العرضية والجوهرية . قوله : ( في محل الصغيرة منها والسر فيه أن النفس لتجردها لا مقدار لها فتكون نسبتها المقادير على السوية . قوله : ( مجتمعة معها) وأما على التعاقب فجائز فإن هيولى خردلة يمكن أن يحل فيها صورة الحبل عندهم بناء على أن الهيولى لا مقدار لها في نفسها كالنفس، فجاز تعاقب الصور الخارجية المتفاوتة بالصغر والكبر عليها جواز تعاقب الصور العقلية المتفاوتة بهما على النفس لكن الاجتماع يجوز في الثانية دون الأولى لما نبهنا في الوجه الأول . قوله : ( لا يجب (زوالها لأن النفس أبدي بالاتفاق . قوله : (سهل استرجاعها يعني أنه قد يكون كذلك لا أن كل صورة عقلية زالت كذلك . قوله : ( لاستحالة بقاء قواها أما المركبات العنصرية فلتداعي البسائط إلى الانفكاك، وأما البسائط فلقبولها الكون والفساد كما نص الشيخ في إلهيات الشفاء على استحالة بقاء الأشخاص العنصرية دائماً . قوله : ( أنَّ الصور العقلية (كلية أي تتصف بالكلية في الملة بخلاف الصورة الخارجية فإنها لا تتصف بها أصلا والمراد بالصورة هي الطبيعة الحاصلة في الذهن مع قطع النظر عن تشخصها الحاصل بسبب المحل الذي قد يطلق عليه المعلوم ،تجوزا فإن المعلوم ما حصل صورته في الذهن لا الصورة الشخصية التي هي علم فإنها لا تتصف بالكيلة كما سيجيء، وما قاله شارح التجريد أن المنطقيين بأسرهم قسموا المفهوم إلى الكلي والجزئي فمعروض الكلية هو المعلوم دون الصورة العقلية التي هي علوم ودون الموجودات الخارجية فمدفوع؛ لأنه إن أراد به أن المعلوم من حيث هو موصوف بالكلية ففاسد فإنهم صرحوا بأن المنطقي يبحث عن المعقولات الثانية ومنها الكلية وإن أراد به أن المعلوم من حيث حصوله في الذهن فهو الصورة
The second type – the sixth aim: The statement of the philosophers regarding intellectual forms. The first form, and likewise the matter imagined with the fiery form, it is impossible for it to be imagined with another form. Rather, intellectual forms are varying in inherence. For the soul, if it is empty of knowledge, its conceptualization of any reality is very difficult, and if it is characterized by some knowledge, its preparedness for the rest increases and its impression by it becomes easy. Second: The large (intellectual form) inheres in the place of the small one together with it. Therefore, the soul is able to imagine the heavens, the earth, the mountains, and small things all at once, unlike material forms, for the large among them does not inhere in the place of the small while being together with it. Third: The weak is not effaced by the strong. That is, the intellectual form of a weak quality does not disappear from the perceiving power due to the occurrence of the form of a strong quality in it, unlike the external, for the weak quality among them is effaced from matter upon the occurrence of the strong quality in it. Fourth: The intellectual form, when it occurs in the intellective [power], its removal is not necessary, and if it is removed, its retrieval is easy without needing to undertake new acquisition, unlike external forms, for they are necessarily removed from elemental matter due to the impossibility of the permanence of their powers forever, and if they are removed, their retrieval requires a cause like the first cause. And among the differences between them is that external forms may be perceived by the external senses unlike intellectual forms, and among them is that intellectual forms are universal unlike external ones. His saying: '(It inheres the large), meaning the form that inheres in a quantitative [thing]. His saying: ('And therefore the soul is able, etc.'), this is based on the fact that the forms of bodily particulars are present in the soul even through the mediation of the senses, as is the verification, as stated by the commentator (may God sanctify his soul) in his glosses on al-Matāliʿ. His saying: ('Together with it'), as for succession, it is permissible based on the fact that prime matter has no magnitude in itself. His saying: ('Easy') meaning in some times. His saying: ('Not mutually exclusive'), and the secret in it is that opposition, similarity, and their like in preventing conjunction are among the rulings of authentic existence, not shadowy existence, as previously stated. His saying: ('And likewise the matter imagined'), he indicated by adducing the two examples that what is meant by external form here is more general than accidental and substantial. His saying: ('In the place of the small among them'), and the secret in it is that the soul, due to its immateriality, has no magnitude, so its relation to all magnitudes is equal. His saying: ('Together with it'), and as for succession, it is permissible, for the prime matter of a mustard seed can have the form of a mountain inhere in it according to them, based on the fact that prime matter has no magnitude in itself like the soul. So the succession of external forms varying in smallness and largeness upon it is permissible just as the succession of intellectual forms varying in them upon the soul is permissible, but conjunction is permissible in the second but not the first due to what we pointed out in the first aspect. His saying: ('Its removal is not necessary') because the soul is eternal by consensus. His saying: ('Easy retrieval') means that it may be so, not that every intellectual form that is removed is like that. His saying: ('Due to the impossibility of the permanence of their powers') – as for elemental compounds, due to the mutual repulsion of the simples towards dissolution, and as for the simples, due to their acceptance of generation and corruption, as the Sheikh stated in the Ilāhiyyāt of al-Shifāʾ regarding the impossibility of the permanence of elemental individuals forever. His saying: ('That the intellectual forms are universal') – that is, they are characterized by universality in the view, unlike the external form, for it is not characterized by it at all. What is meant by form is the nature present in the intellect, disregarding its individuation which occurs due to the locus which may be called the known metaphorically, for the known is what its form is obtained in the intellect, not the individual form which is knowledge, for it is not characterized by universality as will come. And what the commentator of al-Tajrīd said, that the logicians as a whole divided the concept into universal and particular, so the substrate of universality is the known, not the intellectual form which is knowledge and not external existents, is refuted; because if he means that the known insofar as it is described by universality, it is corrupt, for they explicitly stated that the logician investigates second intelligibles, among them universality, and if he means that the known insofar as it is obtained in the intellect, it is the form.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of four characteristics (أمور) of intellectual forms (الصور العقلية) as held by the philosophers (الحكماء), contrasted with external material forms. The author first introduces the topic, then lists four points. First, intellectual forms vary in inherence (الحلول) – the soul's preparedness affects ease of conceptualization. Second, large and small intellectual forms can inhere in the same place simultaneously, unlike material forms where large cannot fit with small. Third, a weak intellectual form is not effaced by a strong one, unlike material forms where the strong effaces the weak. Fourth, intellectual forms are not necessarily removed; if removed, they are easily retrieved without new effort, unlike material forms which inevitably perish and require a new cause for retrieval. Additional differences: material forms can be sensed externally; intellectual forms are universal. The commentary (قوله) elaborates each point: explaining that 'large' here means quantitative form; that the soul's ability to imagine all sizes is based on forms of particulars being present in the soul; that succession of forms in matter is possible due to prime matter's lack of magnitude; that 'easy' retrieval is only sometimes; that non-mutual exclusivity arises because opposition applies to authentic existence not shadowy existence; that 'matter imagined' examples show external form includes substantial and accidental; that the soul's immateriality allows equal relation to all magnitudes; that succession in prime matter is analogous to succession in the soul but conjunction differs; that the soul's eternality explains why removal is not necessary; that 'easy retrieval' is not universal; that elemental forms perish due to repulsion or generation/corruption; and that the universality of intellectual forms is a key distinction, with a refutation of an alternative view on the substrate of universality.

Key Terms

الصور المادية
الحلول
الكبيرة

الزوال
الكلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intellectual forms require physical space and behave like material objects.
Correction: The passage shows that intellectual forms can coexist in the same subject (soul) without size or strength constraints, unlike material forms.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth characteristic, what happens to an intellectual form once it is removed from the intellective power?
A) It is permanently lost.
B) It is easy to retrieve without new acquisition.
C) It requires a new external cause to be retrieved.
D) It cannot be retrieved at all.

Key Takeaway
Intellectual forms possess unique properties—variable inherence, coexistence of different sizes, permanence of weak forms despite strong ones, and ease of retrieval—all of which distinguish them from material forms.

The First Issue on the Universality of 'Humanity': Common Meaning vs Homonymy
Explains that the name 'human' applies to individuals by common meaning not homonymy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the name 'human' applies to individuals by a common meaning rather than by homonymy, and why individualizing characteristics are excluded from that meaning.

Core Idea
The name 'human' shares one meaning across individuals; homonymy would destroy universality.

This passage unpacks the first of two issues about why the intelligible form of 'humanity' is universal. The author clarifies that ‘human’ does not apply to different people like the word ‘ayn’ applies to radically different things (eye, spring, etc.). Instead, it names a single meaning shared by all individuals, stripped of their distinguishing features. Understanding this distinction is the key to grasping how universal concepts work in Islamic logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage (including the commentary line) and its literal translation. Notice that the passage begins by stating this is the first of two issues.
Pay attention to the contrast between 'اشتراك اللفظ' and 'اشتراك معنوي'.
2
Identify the two types of naming
Identify where homonymy (اشتراك اللفظ) is defined and where common meaning (اشتراك معنوي) is defined. Notice that homonymy uses the same word for completely different meanings, while common meaning uses the same word for a single meaning shared by many individuals.
The example 'ayn' clarifies homonymy.
3
Understand what is excluded
Find the sentence that says individualizing characteristics (المشخصات) are not included in the common meaning. Understand that if they were included, the meaning would not be common to all individuals.
These characteristics are accidental attributes and external concomitants.
4
Reflect on why this matters for universals
Consider how this analysis allows the form of 'humanity' to be universal. The rational soul conceives it stripped of individuating details.
This foundational distinction underpins the entire discussion of universals in kalam and logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ) إنهم (ذكروا في معنى كون صورة (الإنسانية) المعقولة (أمراً كلياً أمرين الأول : اسم الإنسان ) مثلاً ( لإفراده ليس باشتراك اللفظ ضرورة مثل : اشتراك لفظ العين بين معانيه التي وضع لفظه بإزاء كل منها على حدة، بل هو ) أي مدلول اسم الإنسان ( معنى مشترك ) بين إفراده وإطلاقه عليها باعتبار ذلك المعنى، وهذا هو الذي يسمى اشتراكاً معنوياً ( ولا يدخل فيه) أي في ذلك المعنى المشترك ( المشخصات ) التي يمتاز بها أفراد بعضها عن بعض، ( وإلا لم يكن ذلك المعنى مشتركاً بين جميع أفراده، بل المشخصات كلها خارجة عنه ( فالنفس) الناطقة ( إذ استحضرت صورة الإنسانية ) أي صورة ذلك المعنى المشترك ( مجردة عن المشخصات ) التي هي عوارض غريبة ولواحق خارجية
قوله: (ليس باشتراك اللفظ ولا من قبيل ما فيه الوضع العام مع خصوص الموضوع له كأسماء الإشارات ونحوها .
Then they mentioned regarding the meaning of the fact that the intelligible form of 'humanity' is a universal matter two issues: The first: the name 'human', for example, for its individuals is not by homonymy necessarily like the homonymy of the word 'ayn' among its meanings for which the word was placed corresponding to each one separately. Rather, it (i.e., the denotation of the name 'human') is a common meaning among its individuals, and its application to them is by virtue of that meaning, and this is what is called common meaning. And the individualizing characteristics by which individuals are distinguished from each other do not enter into that common meaning; otherwise, that meaning would not be common to all its individuals. Rather, all the individualizing characteristics are outside it. So the rational soul, when it recalls the form of 'humanity', i.e., the form of that common meaning, stripped of the individualizing characteristics which are accidental attributes and external concomitants. His saying: ('Not by homonymy') and it is not of the type where there is general positing with particular what is posited for, like demonstratives and the like.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two issues (أمرين) regarding how the intelligible form of 'humanity' (صورة الإنسانية المعقولة) is a universal matter. The author explains that the name 'human' applies to its individuals not by homonymy (اشتراك اللفظ) — where a single word is used for entirely different meanings, like 'ayn' for eye, spring, etc. — but rather by a common meaning (اشتراك معنوي). The name 'human' denotes a single meaning shared by all individuals, and it is applied to them by virtue of that meaning. Individualizing characteristics (المشخصات) that differentiate individuals (e.g., specific height, color) do not enter into this common meaning; otherwise, the meaning would not be shared by all. Thus, the soul (النفس الناطقة) when it conceives the form of 'humanity' conceives it stripped of these individuating accidents. The commentary (قوله) adds that this type is not like cases of general positing with particular subject matter (الوضع العام مع خصوص الموضوع له), such as demonstratives.

Key Terms

اشتراك معنوي
المشخصات

كلية
النفس الناطقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the name 'human' applies to different individuals in the same way that a homonym like 'ayn' applies to different meanings, i.e., completely different senses for each person.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'human' denotes a single meaning common to all individuals, not separate meanings. The differences between individuals are external accidents, not part of the core meaning.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how does the name 'human' apply to its individuals?
A) By homonymy, like 'ayn'.
B) By common meaning shared by all individuals.
C) By general positing with particular subject matter.
D) By accidental attribution.

Key Takeaway
The name 'human' applies to all individuals by a single common meaning, free from their unique distinguishing features.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you interact with people who differ from you in appearance, culture, or status, do you truly see them as sharing the same essential humanity as you, or do you let their individual characteristics define your perception? What would change if you deliberately focused on that common meaning before noticing differences?
?
The rational soul conceives 'humanity' stripped of personal traits. How often do you reflect on your own essence beyond your roles, achievements, and flaws? If you were to describe yourself using only what is universal to all humans, what would you say? How might that shift your priorities today?

Universality as Correspondence of Intellectual Forms
Defines universality of intellectual forms as correspondence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the universality of an intellectual form means it corresponds equally to multiple individuals after abstraction from their particularizing qualities.

Core Idea
A universal intellectual form is a single abstract meaning that fits many individuals.

The philosophers defined 'universal' not as a thing that exists in many places, but as a form that, when stripped of individual specifics, applies equally to multiple individuals. This passage explains that through an example with Zayd, Amr, and Bakr, contrasting with a horse.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author begins by stating that 'intellectual form is universal in the sense that it corresponds to Zayd, Amr, and Bakr up to all its individuals.'
Correspondence (مطابقة) is the key term.
2
Understand abstraction (تجريد)
The author explains that when each individual is presented in imagination and stripped of its specific characteristics (particularizations), the resulting form is the same for all who share that meaning.
Abstraction removes accidental qualities, leaving the common meaning.
3
Follow the example
Imagine Zayd comes to mind first; the soul is affected by the abstract form 'humanity'. Later, when Amr or Bakr appear, the same form is applied, not a new one. The order doesn't matter.
4
Contrast with a different kind
If a horse appears instead, the abstracted form is different (e.g., 'horseness'), not 'humanity'. This clarifies that universality is about commonality within a kind.
This reinforces the definition.
5
Restate the meaning
Now conclude: the intellectual form is universal because it is common to many individuals, in the sense of correspondence after abstraction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني - المقصد السادس : قول الحكماء في الصور العقلية ( كانت ) تلك الصورة كلية على معنى أنها تكون ( مطابقة لـزيـد وعمرو وبـكـر) إلى سائر أفراده والمراد بالمطابقة ما فسره بقوله : ( أي كل واحد ) من تلك الأفراد ( إذا ) حضر في الخيال و (جرد عن مشخصاته كانت تلك الصورة أعني صورة المعنى المشترك ( هي بعينها ( الأثر ( الحاصل منه ) ، أي من ذلك الواحد الذي جرد مشخصاته لا تختلف تلك الصورة باختلاف الأفراد التي تجرد عن المشخصات، حتى إذا سبق واحد منها إلى النفس فتأثرت منه بذلك الأثر المجرد عن العوارض لم يكن لما عداه من الأفراد إذا حضر عندها تأثير آخر، وإذا كان هذا المتأخر سابقاً انعكس الحال بينهما، ولو كان الحاضر من غير أفراده كفرس مثلاً لكان الأثر الحاصل في القوة العاقلة بالتجريد عن المشخصات صورة أخرى سوى صورة الإنسان، فهذا معنى كون الصورة العقلية كلية مشتركة بين كثيرين
The second type - the sixth objective: The statement of the philosophers about intellectual forms. (It was) that form universal in the sense that it corresponds to Zayd, Amr, and Bakr up to all its individuals. And what is meant by correspondence is what he explained by saying: (i.e., each one) of those individuals (when) it is present in the imagination and (stripped of its particularizations, then that form—I mean the form of the common meaning—(is exactly the same as) the (effect (obtained from it)) i.e., from that one which is stripped of its particularizations. That form does not differ with the difference of the individuals from which the particularizations are stripped. So that if one of them precedes to the soul and it is affected by that effect stripped of accidents, then no other effect is from the other individuals when they are present before it. And if this latter were preceded, the situation would be reversed. And if the present were from non-its individuals, like a horse for example, then the effect obtained in the rational faculty by stripping of particularizations would be another form other than the form of man. So this is the meaning of the intellectual form being universal, common among many.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage defines what is meant by saying that an intellectual form (الصورة العقلية) is universal (كلية). It is not a claim that the same form exists in multiple places or is divisible. Rather, universality means that when a form is abstracted (تجريد) from all particularizing qualities (مشخصات) of an individual (like Zayd's specific height, color, etc.), the resulting abstract meaning—say, 'humanity'—corresponds equally to every individual that shares that meaning. The author illustrates this: if Zayd's form is abstracted and impressed in the mind, then when Amr and Bakr are later imagined, the same abstract form (not a new one) is applied to them. If the order were reversed, the same form would apply. But if a completely different kind (like a horse) were imagined, the abstract form would be different (e.g., 'horseness'). Thus 'universal' here means 'common (مشترك) to many' in the sense of correspondence (مطابقة), not in the sense of a single entity existing in many places.

Key Terms
صورة عقلية
كلية
مطابقة
تجريد
مشخصات
عوارض
معنى مشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Universality means the form exists in multiple locations simultaneously, like a Platonic Form.
Correction: The passage clarifies that universality means correspondence after abstraction, not multiple existence. The same abstract meaning applies to many individuals, but the form itself is not spatially multiplied.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is meant by saying an intellectual form is universal?
A) It exists in multiple locations simultaneously.
B) It can be divided into parts.
C) It corresponds equally to multiple individuals when stripped of particularizations.
D) It is a Platonic Form existing in a separate realm.

Key Takeaway
The universality of an intellectual form is defined as its correspondence, after abstraction from particularizations, to all individuals sharing that common meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you interact with others, do you see them first as individuals with unique stories, or do you immediately reduce them to a category (e.g., 'student', 'rich', 'poor')? How might that affect your compassion and justice?
?
The philosophers say that the universal form is obtained by stripping away accidental qualities. In your own life, have you ever dismissed someone because of their outward differences, forgetting the common humanity you share? What concrete step can you take this week to see beyond superficial particularities?
?
If all humans share the same abstract form of humanity, what does that demand of you in terms of how you treat others—especially those you dislike or disagree with? Are you truly honoring that common meaning, or are you blinded by accidental differences that have nothing to do with their essence?

The Particularity and Universality of Intellectual Forms
Raises and answers the objection that an intellectual form is particular yet claimed universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an intellectual form can be both particular (due to its mental accidents) and universal (when considered in itself) without contradiction.

Core Idea
Universality and particularity can coexist when viewed from different aspects.

This passage addresses a key objection in Islamic epistemology: how can a mental concept (intellectual form) be universal if it exists as a particular mental accident? The author explains that the form's universality lies in its meaning when stripped of its mental particulars, while its particularity arises from its occurrence in a specific mind.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection states that the intellectual form is particular because it resides in a particular soul and is subject to mental accidents. So how can it be universal?
Notice the key terms: صورة عقلية, جزئية, كلية.
2
Identify the answer's core distinction
The answer distinguishes between the form taken in itself (universal) and the form with its mental accidents (particular). Universality is about correspondence to many individuals when accidents are ignored.
The phrase 'إذا أخذت في نفسها' is crucial.
3
Reflect on the added refinement
The author adds that if the form (in itself) were to exist externally, it would be identical to an individual. This reinforces that universality does not contradict particularity because they are different aspects.
Consider how this answers the objection comprehensively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : لا شك أن الصورة العقلية الإنسانية الحالة في القوة العاقلة صورة جزئية معروضة لعوارض ذهنية باعتبار حلولها في نفس جزئية، ولذلك امتازت عن الصورة الإنسانية الحالة في نفس أخرى فكيف تكون كلية مع كونها جزئية أيضاً؟ قلت : لا منافاة لأن كليتها باعتبار أنَّها إذا أخذت في نفسها لا مع عوارضها الذهنية طابقت الأمور الكثيرة، كما مر، ومن ثمة زيد في المطابقة شيء آخر وهو أن تلك الصورة المأخوذة من الحيثية المذكورة إذا فرضت في الخارج متشخصة بتشخص فرد من أفرادها، كانت عين ذلك الفرد ومن البين أنَّ كليتها بهذا المعنى لا تنافي جزئيتها من حيث أنها محفوفة بمشخصات ذهنية عارضة لها بواسطة محلها
For if you say: There is no doubt that the human intellectual form residing in the rational faculty is a particular form subject to mental accidents by virtue of its occurrence in a particular soul, and therefore it is distinguished from the human form residing in another soul; so how can it be universal while also being particular? I say: There is no contradiction because its universality is by virtue that when it is taken in itself, not with its mental accidents, it corresponds to many things, as has passed. And from there another thing was added to the correspondence, namely that that form taken from the aforementioned perspective, if it were assumed in the external world individualized by the individuation of one of its individuals, it would be that same individual. And it is evident that its universality in this sense does not contradict its particularity in that it is surrounded by mental particularities accidental to it by virtue of its substrate.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection: The intellectual form of humanity is a particular (جزئية) form because it resides in a particular mind (قوة عاقلة) and is subject to mental accidents (عوارض ذهنية). So how can it be universal (كلية) when it is also particular? The answer: There is no conflict. The form is universal when considered in itself (في نفسها) apart from its mental accidents. When so considered, it corresponds (يطابق) to many individuals. The author adds another refinement: if that form (taken in itself) were to exist externally, individuated by the individuation (تشخص) of a specific individual, it would be identical to that individual. Thus, the form's universality (in the sense of correspondence) does not negate its particularity due to the mental particularities (مشخصات ذهنية) that accompany it because of its substrate (محلها). The key point is that universality and particularity are not mutually exclusive when one considers different aspects: the form in itself vs. the form as enveloped in mental accidents.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A particular mental image cannot be universal because it is a specific instance.
Correction: Universality pertains to the meaning of the form when considered apart from its mental accidents; particularity is due to those accidents, so they are compatible.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection that the intellectual form is particular and therefore cannot be universal?
A) He denies that the form is particular.
B) He distinguishes between the form considered in itself (universal) and the form with mental accidents (particular).
C) He says universality applies only to external objects.
D) He claims the form is both particular and universal in the same respect.

Key Takeaway
An intellectual form can be universal when considered in itself, independent of the mental accidents that make it particular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your own understanding of a concept—like 'justice' or 'mercy'—is the only correct one, forgetting that your personal experiences have shaped it in a particular way? This lesson shows that a universal meaning can coexist with particular accidental features. How can you humbly hold onto the universal truth while acknowledging your own limited perspective? Commit to regularly questioning whether your interpretation is truly universal or merely a particular instance colored by your own soul.
?
When you encounter someone who disagrees with you on a matter of creed, do you immediately dismiss their view because it seems 'particular' or different from yours? This passage teaches that universality is about correspondence to reality when stripped of mental accidents. This means our own 'mental accidents' (biases, upbringing, emotions) can obscure the universal truth. This week, when you feel a strong disagreement, pause and ask: 'Am I reacting to the universal meaning or to my own mental accidents?' and then seek the pure, abstracted form of the issue.

Secondary Objection: Each Individual Would Be Universal
Responds to objection that each individual would be universal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to the correspondence theory of knowledge and its refutation, clarifying why external individuals remain particulars despite sharing a universal form.

Core Idea
Correspondence does not turn particulars into universals.

This lesson addresses a subtle objection: if the intellectual form corresponds to each external individual, then by reciprocity each individual would correspond to the form and hence to all other individuals, making each individual universal. The answer distinguishes between external existence and shadowy existence, showing that only the quiddities in the mind are universal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the objection that begins with 'It should not be said...'. Notice how the objection uses the idea that correspondence is mutual (بين بين) to argue that each individual would correspond to all others.
The objection is based on a seemingly logical symmetry.
2
Analyze the answer's distinction
The answer distinguishes between 'external existence' (وجود خارجي) and 'shadowy existence' (وجود ظلي). Understand that external existents are all particulars, while the quiddities in the mind are universal because they are identical with the quiddity of the rooted individuals.
This distinction is key to refuting the objection.
3
Reflect on the warning
The author ends with 'So reflect, for it is one of the slippery points.' Consider why this distinction is subtle and easy to confuse.
Be careful not to mix the perspectives of particularity and universality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : كما أن الصورة العقلية تطابق أفرادها الخارجية كذلك كل واحد منها يطابقها لأن المطابقة لا تتصور إلا بين بين فيلزم أن يكون كل فرد مطابقاً لسائر الأفراد أيضاً ضرورة اشتراكها في مطابقة أمر واحد، فيكون كل فرد كلياً بالمعنى الذي ذكرتموه لأنا نقول : ليست المطابقة [؟] النظر عن التشخص الذهني كلية ومن حيث تشخصها جزئية فالموجودات بالوجود الخارجي أي الأصلي كلها جزيئات سواء كانت موجودة في الأعيان أو قائمة بالأذهان قيام الأعراض بمحالها، وهي الصور الجزئية العلمية المشخصة بتشخيص المحال والموجودات بالوجود الظلي أعني الماهيات المحفوظة في تلك الصور القائمة بالمحال كلية بمعنى اتحادها مع الماهية التي للأفراد المتأصلة في الوجود أعياناً كانت أو صوراً، فتأمل فإنه من المزالق
It should not be said: Just as the intellectual form corresponds to its external individuals, likewise each one of them corresponds to it, because correspondence is only conceivable between [two things]. So it would follow that each individual corresponds to the other individuals as well, by necessity of their sharing in correspondence to one thing. Thus each individual would be universal in the sense you mentioned. For we say: The correspondence is not [؟] setting aside the mental particularization, it is universal; and from the perspective of its particularization, it is particular. So existents by external existence, i.e., original existence, are all particulars, whether they exist in concrete entities or subsist in minds as the subsistence of accidents in their substrates—these are the particular scientific forms particularized by the particularization of the substrate. And existents by shadowy existence, i.e., the quiddities preserved in those forms subsisting in the substrates, are universal in the sense of their union with the quiddity that belongs to the individuals rooted in existence, whether as concrete entities or as forms. So reflect, for it is one of the slippery points.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its answer. The objection: If the intellectual form corresponds (يطابق) to each external individual, then by reciprocity each individual also corresponds to the form. Since correspondence is mutual (بين بين), each individual would correspond to all other individuals that share the same form, making each individual universal in the same sense. The answer: The correspondence is not mutual in that way. The intellectual form, considered apart from mental particularization, is universal. The individuals themselves, as external existents (وجود خارجي), are all particulars. However, the 'shadowy existence' (وجود ظلي) of the quiddities (ماهيات) preserved in the intellectual forms are universal because they are identical (اتحاد) with the quiddity that the individuals have in their rooted existence (وجود متأصل). In summary: external individuals are particular; the shadowy quiddities in the mind are universal. The author warns that this is a slippery point (مزالق) requiring careful reflection.

جزيئات
وجود ظلي
ماهيات
اتحاد
وجود متأصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Correspondence between form and individuals is symmetric, so individuals become universal.
Correction: The form is universal only when considered apart from particularization; individuals remain particulars in external existence.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what is the status of external individuals in terms of universality?
A) They are universal because they correspond to the form.
B) They are particulars in external existence.
C) They are both universal and particular.
D) They do not exist independently.

Key Takeaway
External individuals are always particulars; universality belongs only to the quiddities as they exist in the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author calls this 'one of the slippery points.' Where in your own thinking do you slip into assuming that the correspondence between your mind and reality is perfect and symmetric? What do you need to change to honor the distinction between mental concepts and external realities?
?
You carry mental pictures of many things: your own identity, your beliefs, your goals. How many of these are you treating as universal truths when they might be particular to your limited perspective? Will you examine at least one today and ask: Is this a universal quiddity or a particular image in my mind?

Critique of the Author's Statements on Universality and Correspondence
A critical analysis of the author's contradictory statements on universality and correspondence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator identifies and analyzes a contradiction between the author's main text and his glosses regarding the definitions and applicability of universality as commonness and correspondence.

Core Idea
The author's glosses contradict his main text on whether mental accidents affect the universality of intellectual forms.

This passage examines a subtle but important inconsistency in the author's own writings. The commentator compares what the author says in the main text with what he wrote in his glosses on al-Tajrid. By analyzing the two meanings of universality—commonness and correspondence—the commentator shows that the two statements cannot be reconciled, and he suggests that further reflection is needed or that the author may have changed his view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two statements being compared
Read the main text's answer (about no contradiction) and the glosses' position. Notice the commentator says they do not conform.
The glosses are on al-Tajrid, a key work.
2
Understand the two meanings of universality
The passage distinguishes universality as commonness (ishtirak) and as correspondence (mutabaqa). Note what each applies to: commonness applies neither to external objects nor intellectual forms; correspondence applies to intellectual forms even with mental accidents.
3
Grasp the contradiction
If correspondence applies with mental accidents, then a form would be both universal and particular from the same aspect, which is impossible. This shows the glosses contradict the main text.
The commentator suggests an alternative argument that would avoid this.
4
Examine the added remark
The second part explains why an additional phrase was inserted: because mental accidents are not taken into account in universality, making the statement correct.
This ties back to the earlier discussion in the first objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : قلت: لا منافاة لأن كليتهما إلخ) تقرير الجواب هاهنا لا يلائم كلامه في حواشي التجريد، فإنه صرح هناك بأنَّ الكلية بمعنى الاشتراك لا تعرض للموجودات الخارجية وهو ظاهر، ولا للصور العقلية لأن كل واحدة منها صورة جزئية في نفس جزئية فامتنع اشتراكها، وأما الكلية بمعنى المطابقة فتعرض للصور العقلية، إذ المفهوم من هذا الكلام أنَّ المطابقة بالمعنى المذكور تعرض للصور العقلية، ولو أخذت مع عوارضها الذهنية، فإنه لو حمل كلامه على عروضها لها مأخوذة في نفسها لا مع عوارضها الذهنية اتجه أن الاشتراك يعرض لها أيضاً مأخوذة، كذلك ألا أن یری الصورة العالمية مأخوذة كذلك هي مفهوم الحيوان على تقدير اتحاد العلم والمعلوم، وهذا المفهوم مشترك بين كثيرين بمعنى تحقق حصة منه في كل منها فتعين ان مراده هناك عروض المطابقة للصور العقلية مع أنها صورة جزئية في نفس جزئية، ولهذا اعترض عليه بأنه يستلزم أن يكون أمر واحد من جهة واحدة كلياً وجزئياً أيضاً، فلا يكون مفهوما الجزئية والكلية متقابلين ولم يقل به أحد، ثم قيل : ولو استدل على عدم صحة تفسير الكلية بالمطابقة بالمعنى المذكور بأن المطابقة بهذا المعنى تعرض للصور العقلية والكلية لا يمكن عروضها لتلك الصور، لكونها صورة جزئية حالة في نفس جزئية لكان صواباً، فليتأمل. قوله : ( ومن ثمة زيد في المطابقة أي ومن أجل أنَّ العوارض الذهنية ليست مأخوذة في الكلية قيل ذلك وصح هذا القول إذ لو كانت مأخوذة فيها لم يمكن وجود تلك الصورة في الخارج كما سبق إليه الإشارة في أثناء المقصد الأول ولو فرض وقوع هذا المستحيل لم يلزم أن يكون عين ذلك الفرد بل لم يجز
His statement: 'I say: There is no contradiction because its universality, etc.' The formulation of the answer here does not conform to his words in the Glosses on al-Tajrid (Hawashi al-Tajrid). For he stated there that universality in the sense of commonness (ishtirak) does not apply to external existents—and that is evident—nor to intellectual forms because each one of them is a particular form in a particular soul, so their commonness is impossible. But universality in the sense of correspondence applies to intellectual forms, for the understanding from this speech is that correspondence in the mentioned sense applies to intellectual forms even if they are taken with their mental accidents. For if his words are interpreted as applying to them taken in themselves not with their mental accidents, then commonness would also apply to them taken likewise. Except that the universal form taken thus is the concept of animal on the assumption of the unity of knowledge and the known. And this concept is common among many in the sense of a portion of it being realized in each. So it is determined that his intention there is the application of correspondence to intellectual forms even though they are particular forms in particular souls. For this reason, objection was made against him that it entails that one thing from one aspect is both universal and particular, and then the concepts of particularity and universality would not be opposites, and no one has said that. Then it was said: And if one were to argue for the incorrectness of interpreting universality as correspondence in the mentioned sense—because correspondence in this sense applies to intellectual forms, but universality cannot apply to those forms because they are particular forms subsisting in a particular soul—that would be correct. So let it be pondered. His statement: 'And from there another thing was added to the correspondence' means: because the mental accidents are not taken into account in universality, that was said and this statement is correct. For if they were taken into account, it would not be possible for that form to exist externally, as was indicated in the course of the first objective, and if this impossible were supposed to occur, it would not follow that it would be identical to that individual; rather it would not be permissible.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the author's own statements. It points out an inconsistency between the answer given in the main text and what the author wrote in his glosses on al-Tajrid (حواشي التجريد). In the glosses, he said that universality as commonness (اشتراك) does not apply either to external things or to intellectual forms (since each is particular). But universality as correspondence (مطابقة) does apply to intellectual forms, even when taken with their mental accidents. This contradicts the answer in the main text, which restricted correspondence to forms taken in themselves. If correspondence applied even with accidents, then the form would be both universal and particular from the same aspect, making the two concepts not opposite—which no one accepts. The passage further comments on the added remark 'from there another thing was added' (ومن ثمة زيد), explaining that it was said because mental accidents are not considered in universality. If they were, the form could not exist externally, and if it hypothetically did, it wouldn't be identical to the individual. The author thus seems to have two positions, and the commentator (شارح) is noting the need for careful reflection (فليتأمل).

The Universality and Particularity of Mental Forms
Objection and answer on universality and particularity of mental forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers reconcile the apparent contradiction between the universality and particularity of mental forms by considering different perspectives.

Core Idea
Mental forms are universal in essence, particular in existence.

A central problem in Islamic philosophy is how mental forms (concepts) can be universal when they exist in a particular mind. This passage presents an objection from critics and the philosophers' answer, along with a further subtle objection and its resolution. The discussion clarifies the distinction between the form itself and its mental accidents, as well as between original and shadow existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and its English translation carefully. Notice the structure: it starts with an objection, then an answer, then a further objection, and another answer.
The passage is dense; do not worry about every detail at first.
2
Identify the main objection and answer
Pinpoint how the philosophers answer the first objection: universality is when the form is considered without mental accidents; particularity is due to its mental individuators.
The key is the phrase 'when they are taken in themselves without their mental accidents'.
3
Understand the sub-objection and its answer
The sub-objection argues that if correspondence is reciprocal, each individual would be universal. The answer distinguishes between the form's universality (apart from individuation) and its particularity (with individuation).
Note the distinction between 'external existence' and 'shadow existence'.
4
Reflect on the concluding warning
The author says 'so reflect, for it is among slippery places.' This indicates the subtlety. Contemplate how the same quiddity can be both universal in the mind and particular in external objects.
This summarises the entire lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني - المقصد السادس : قول الحكماء في الصور العقلية الإنسانية الحالة في نفس أخرى فكيف تكون كلية مع كونها جزئية أيضاً؟ قلت : لا منافاة لأن كليتها باعتبار أنَّها إذا أخذت في نفسها لا مع عوارضها الذهنية طابقت الأمور الكثيرة، كما مر، ومن ثمة زيد في المطابقة شيء آخر وهو أن تلك الصورة المأخوذة من الحيثية المذكورة إذا فرضت في الخارج متشخصة بتشخص فرد من أفرادها، كانت عين ذلك الفرد ومن البين أنَّ كليتها بهذا المعنى لا تنافي جزئيتها من حيث أنها محفوفة بمشخصات ذهنية عارضة لها بواسطة محلها، لا يقال : كما أن الصورة العقلية تطابق أفرادها الخارجية كذلك كل واحد منها يطابقها لأن المطابقة لا تتصور إلا بين بين فيلزم أن يكون كل فرد مطابقاً لسائر الأفراد أيضاً ضرورة اشتراكها في مطابقة أمر واحد، فيكون كل فرد كلياً بالمعنى الذي ذكرتموه لأنا نقول : ليست النظر عن التشخص الذهني كلية ومن حيث تشخصها جزئية فالموجودات بالوجود الخارجي أي الأصلي كلها جزيئات سواء كانت موجودة في الأعيان أو قائمة بالأذهان قيام الأعراض بمحالها، وهي الصور الجزئية العلمية المشخصة بتشخيص المحال والموجودات بالوجود الظلي أعني الماهيات المحفوظة في تلك الصور القائمة بالمحال كلية بمعنى اتحادها مع الماهية التي للأفراد المتأصلة في الوجود أعياناً كانت أو صوراً، فتأمل فإنه من المزالق .
The second type - the sixth objective: The saying of the philosophers about human mental forms (al-suwar al-'aqliyyah) that inhere in a particular soul: How can they be universal while being particular as well? I say: There is no contradiction because their universality (kulliyyah) is by virtue of the fact that when they are taken in themselves without their mental accidents (a'waridhiha al-dhihniyyah), they correspond (tabiq) to many things, as has passed. And for that reason another thing was added to correspondence (mutabaqah): that that form taken from the mentioned respect, if it is supposed in the external world as individuated by the individuation (tashakhkhus) of an individual of its individuals, it would be that very individual. And it is clear that its universality in this sense does not contradict its particularity (juz'iyyah) in that it is surrounded by mental individuators (mushakhkhasat dhihniyyah) that are accidental to it by means of its substrate. It might not be said: Just as the mental form corresponds to its external individuals, likewise each one of them corresponds to it, because correspondence is only conceived between two things; then it would follow that each individual also corresponds to the other individuals necessarily because of their sharing in corresponding to one thing. Thus each individual would be universal in the sense you mentioned. For we say: It is not the consideration apart from mental individuation that is universal, and with respect to its individuation it is particular. So existents by external existence (al-wujud al-khariji), i.e., original existence, are all particulars, whether they exist in external objects (al-a'yan) or subsist in minds (al-adhhan) in the manner of accidents in their substrates. And those are the particular scientific forms individuated by the individuation of substrates. And the existents by shadow existence (al-wujud al-zilli)—I mean the quiddities (mahiyyat) preserved in those forms that subsist in substrates—are universal in the sense of their identity with the quiddity that belongs to the individuals that are rooted in existence, whether external objects or forms. So reflect, for it is among slippery places.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection is: How can mental forms (al-suwar al-'aqliyyah) be universal (kulliyyah) while they are particular (juz'iyyah) due to existing in a particular soul? The answer: There is no contradiction because universality is considered when the form is taken in itself without its mental accidents (al-a'waridh al-dhihniyyah); in that case it corresponds to many individuals. Particularity is from the perspective of its being surrounded by mental individuators (al-mushakhkhasat al-dhihniyyah) that are accidental to it via its substrate. Then a further objection is raised: If the mental form corresponds to external individuals, then each external individual would also correspond to the form, and since correspondence is reciprocal, each individual would correspond to all other individuals (since they share in corresponding to the same form), making each individual universal. The answer is that the mental form is universal only when considered apart from mental individuation, and particular when considered with it. External existents (al-mawjudat bi al-wujud al-khariji) are all particular, whether in the external world or in minds as accidents. The scientific forms (al-suwar al-'ilmiyyah) that subsist in minds are also particular due to their substrate. However, existents by shadow existence (al-wujud al-zilli)—the quiddities (al-mahiyyat) preserved in those forms—are universal in the sense of being identical with the quiddity of the individuals that have genuine existence. The passage ends with a warning that this is a subtle point.

Key Terms
الصور العقلية
الكلية
الجزئية
المطابقة
العوارض الذهنية
الوجود الظلي
التشخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Universality and particularity cannot coexist in the same entity.
Correction: They can coexist because they are considered from different perspectives: universality pertains to the essence without accidents, particularity pertains to the individuated existence.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what is the basis of the universality of mental forms?
A) Their existence in the mind
B) Their consideration in themselves without mental accidents
C) Their correspondence to many individuals
D) Their being in a particular soul

Key Takeaway
Mental forms are universal in their essence (quiddity) and particular in their individuated existence, a distinction that resolves the apparent contradiction.

How does this awareness affect the seriousness with which you seek to purify your understanding of such concepts, knowing your individual grasp is limited yet corresponds to a greater reality?
?
The author warns that this topic is 'among slippery places'. In what areas of your religious understanding might you be blurring the distinction between the universal and the particular, perhaps causing confusion or error? What specific step can you take today to ensure your knowledge of Allah's attributes is not tainted by your own limited mental individuations?
?

Discrepancy on Universality: Kulliyyah as Participation and Correspondence
Commentator notes discrepancy with Hawashi al-Tajrid on universality of mental forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's critique of the inconsistency between the current answer and the author's position in Hawashi al-Tajrid regarding the application of universality to mental forms.

Core Idea
The commentator reveals a tension in the author's own treatment of universality as participation versus correspondence.

When a scholar's own statements seem to conflict, a careful commentator must reconcile or expose the inconsistency. Here, the commentator points out that the answer in the current passage does not align with what the author wrote in his Hawashi al-Tajrid. By following the logic, we will see how the concept of universality (kulliyyah) in its two senses—participation (ishtirak) and correspondence (mutabaqah)—leads to a problematic conclusion when applied to mental forms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the commentator's argument. Note the two senses of universality mentioned.
2
Identify the author's positions
Distinguish the author's position on universality in Hawashi al-Tajrid for each sense: participation does not attach to mental forms; correspondence does attach.
The commentator explicitly states these positions.
3
Follow the commentator's analysis
The commentator explores two interpretations: taking mental forms with or without their accidents, and shows the problems with each.
This leads to the objection that one thing would be both universal and particular.
4
Understand the suggested alternative
The commentator suggests that a better argument is simply to deny that correspondence attaches to mental forms at all, avoiding the contradiction.
5
Reflect on the larger lesson
Consider why consistency matters in theological works and how careful analysis can resolve apparent contradictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : قلت: لا منافاة لأن كليتهما إلخ) تقرير الجواب هاهنا لا يلائم كلامه في حواشي التجريد، فإنه صرح هناك بأنَّ الكلية بمعنى الاشتراك لا تعرض للموجودات الخارجية وهو ظاهر، ولا للصور العقلية لأن كل واحدة منها صورة جزئية في نفس جزئية فامتنع اشتراكها، وأما الكلية بمعنى المطابقة فتعرض للصور العقلية، إذ المفهوم من هذا الكلام أنَّ المطابقة بالمعنى المذكور تعرض للصور العقلية، ولو أخذت مع عوارضها الذهنية، فإنه لو حمل كلامه على عروضها لها مأخوذة في نفسها لا مع عوارضها الذهنية اتجه أن الاشتراك يعرض لها أيضاً مأخوذة، كذلك ألا أنَّ یری الصورة العالمية مأخوذة كذلك هي مفهوم الحيوان على تقدير اتحاد العلم والمعلوم، وهذا المفهوم مشترك بين كثيرين بمعنى تحقق حصة منه في كل منها فتعين ان مراده هناك عروض المطابقة للصور العقلية مع أنها صورة جزئية في نفس جزئية، ولهذا اعترض عليه بأنه يستلزم أن يكون أمر واحد من جهة واحدة كلياً وجزئياً أيضاً، فلا يكون مفهوما الجزئية والكلية متقابلين ولم يقل به أحد، ثم قيل : ولو استدل على عدم صحة تفسير الكلية بالمطابقة بالمعنى المذكور بأن المطابقة بهذا المعنى تعرض للصور العقلية والكلية لا يمكن عروضها لتلك الصور، لكونها صورة جزئية حالة في نفس جزئية لكان صواباً، فليتأمل.
His saying: 'I say: There is no contradiction because their universality, etc.': The formulation of the answer here is not compatible with his statement in Hawashi al-Tajrid. For he explicitly stated there that universality (kulliyyah) in the sense of participation (ishtirak) does not attach to external existents—this is clear—nor to mental forms (al-suwar al-'aqliyyah) because each one of them is a particular form (surah juz'iyyah) in a particular soul, so their participation is impossible. As for universality in the sense of correspondence (mutabaqah), it attaches to mental forms. For the understanding from this speech is that correspondence in the mentioned sense attaches to mental forms, even if they are taken with their mental accidents. For if his speech is interpreted as their having it (i.e., correspondence) when taken in themselves without their mental accidents, then participation could also attach to them when taken likewise. However, one sees the universal form taken likewise—it is the concept of animal on the assumption of the identity of knowledge and known. And this concept is shared among many in the sense that a portion (hissah) of it is realized in each of them. So it is determined that his intent there is the attachment of correspondence to mental forms despite their being a particular form in a particular soul. For this reason, he was objected to that it entails that one thing from one aspect is both universal and particular, so the concepts of particularity and universality would not be opposites, and no one has said this. Then it was said: If one were to argue against the validity of interpreting universality as correspondence in the mentioned sense, by saying that correspondence in this sense attaches to mental forms, while universality cannot attach to those forms because they are a particular form subsisting in a particular soul, it would be correct. So let one reflect.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the previous answer. The commentator notes that the answer given here does not align with the author's statement in his Hawashi al-Tajrid. In Hawashi al-Tajrid, the author explicitly said that universality (kulliyyah) in the sense of participation (ishtirak) does not attach to external existents nor to mental forms, because each mental form is a particular form in a particular soul. However, universality in the sense of correspondence (mutabaqah) does attach to mental forms. The commentator analyzes the possibilities: if we interpret the author's claim as meaning that correspondence attaches even when mental forms are taken with their accidents, then that leads to the problem that the same thing would be both universal and particular from one aspect. If we interpret it as meaning that correspondence attaches when forms are taken in themselves, then participation could also attach, which contradicts the earlier claim. The commentator resolves that the author's intent in Hawashi al-Tajrid was that correspondence attaches to mental forms despite their particularity, which invites the objection that this makes universality and particularity not opposites. The commentator concludes that a better argument would be to simply deny that universality (as correspondence) can attach to mental forms at all, because they are particular.

Correction: The commentator shows that the author's own statements conflict: in Hawashi al-Tajrid, universality as participation is denied for mental forms, while universality as correspondence is affirmed, leading to a logical contradiction that needs resolution.

Universality as correspondence and its application to real species
Explanation of universality as correspondence for real species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that universality (kulliyyah) means correspondence of an ideal essence to its external counterpart, and how this applies primarily to real species.

Core Idea
Universality is the correspondence of an ideal shadow to a real thing.

In this passage, the commentator refines the concept of universality (kulliyyah). He argues that it is not simply any correspondence, but specifically the correspondence of an ideal essence (mithaliyyah) that exists only as a shadow of its external referent. This becomes clearest when we consider real species (such as 'human'), and he explains how to extend this understanding to other types of universals by treating their parts as notional individuals. Let's examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its English translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument: first a clarification, then the definition of universality, then its application.
2
Understand the definition of universality
Notice that universality is defined as correspondence of an ideal essence (dhat mithaliyyah) that is not rooted in existence but is a shadow (zill) of something external. This distinguishes it from mere correspondence.
The commentator emphasizes that mental accidents are not included in universality.
3
Apply to real species and other universals
The passage states that this correspondence is most evident in real species. For other universals (like genera), we can either treat their parts as portions (hisas) that are notional individuals, or abstract from individuators to see the common meaning.
This shows the flexibility and nuance of the concept.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ومن ثمة زيد في المطابقة أي ومن أجل أنَّ العوارض الذهنية ليست مأخوذة في الكلية قيل ذلك وصح هذا القول إذ لو كانت مأخوذة فيها لم يمكن وجود تلك الصورة في الخارج كما سبق إليه الإشارة في أثناء المقصد الأول ولو فرض وقوع هذا المستحيل لم يلزم أن يكون عين ذلك الفرد بل لم يجز. بل عن الكلية عبارة عن المطابقة مطلقاً بل مطابقة ذات مثالية غير متأصلة في الوجود لما هي ظل لها، واعلم أنَّ ما ذكر في تصوير المطابقة التي هي معنى الكلية، إنما يظهر في الكليات التي هي أنواع حقيقية، فإذا أريد أجزاؤه في سائر الكليات، قيست إلى حصصها التي هي أفرادها الاعتبارية، فإنها أنواع حقيقية بالقياس إليها، أو جعل ما عدا المعنى المشترك بين أفرادها بمنزلة المشخصات في التجريد عنها
His saying: 'And for that reason another thing was added to correspondence,' i.e., because mental accidents are not taken into account in universality, that was said, and this statement is correct. For if they were taken into account, it would be impossible for that form to exist externally, as has been previously indicated in the first objective. And if this impossible were supposed to occur, it would not necessarily be that very individual; rather it would not be permissible. Rather, universality (kulliyyah) is nothing but correspondence (mutabaqah) in an absolute sense, but rather a correspondence of an ideal (mithaliyyah) essence that is not rooted in existence to that which it is a shadow (zill) for it. And know that what was mentioned in the depiction of correspondence, which is the meaning of universality, only appears in universals that are real species (anwa' haqiqiyyah). So if its parts (ajza') are intended in other universals, they are measured against their portions (hisas) which are their notional individuals (afrad i'tibariyyah), for they are real species in relation to them. Or that which is other than the common meaning among its individuals is treated like the individuators (mushakhkhasat) by abstracting (tajrid) from them.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the meaning of universality (kulliyyah) as correspondence (mutabaqah). It begins by clarifying a previous point: the reason a second thing was added to correspondence is that mental accidents are not included in the consideration of universality. If they were, the form could not exist externally. Then it redefines universality: it is not just any correspondence, but specifically the correspondence of an ideal (mithaliyyah) essence that has no independent existence in the external world—it is a shadow (zill) of something else. The passage then notes that this depiction of correspondence as the meaning of universality is primarily evident in real species (al-anwa' al-haqiqiyyah), such as 'human' or 'animal'. For other types of universals (like genera), we consider their parts (ajza') as portions (hisas) that are their notional individuals (afrad i'tibariyyah), making them real species relative to those individuals. Alternatively, we can abstract (tajrid) from the individuating factors and treat the universal concept apart from its specific differences.

Second Aspect of Universality and Two Views on Mental Forms
Second aspect of universality and two groups on mental forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second aspect of universality and the two competing views on mental forms—whether they are identical to the quiddities or mere likenesses—and how this affects the reality of universality.

Core Idea
Universality applies to known things through forms; its reality depends on the nature of mental forms.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif presents a pivotal epistemological debate about mental forms. It begins with a second aspect of universality: what is known through a mental form is universal, not the form itself. The author then explains that this claim only coheres with one of two views on mental forms—the view that forms are identical to the quiddities of known things. The alternative view, that forms are mere shadows differing in essence, forces universality to be merely metaphorical. The lesson unpacks these positions and their implications, including a supporting gloss from al-Farabi.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice that the passage includes both the main argument and interwoven glosses (commentary). Focus on the opening statement about the second aspect of universality.
The passage is dense; take your time to distinguish the main text from the glosses.
2
Identify the two groups on mental forms
The passage clearly distinguishes two groups: one claiming mental forms are identical in essence (equal in mahiyyah) to known things, and another claiming they are mere likenesses (ashbah) differing in essence. Understand the difference.
3
Connect universality to each group
For the first group, universality truly attaches to mental forms because they are the quiddities themselves. For the second group, describing the form as universal is metaphorical, since the form is only a shadow. This is the core of the second aspect.
4
Examine the al-Farabi gloss
Read the gloss quoting al-Farabi: the true object of perception is the mental form itself; the external thing is known indirectly. This supports the first group's view and avoids infinite regress.
This gloss reinforces the idea that knowledge and known are identical in essence.
5
Summarize the epistemological divergence
Conclude that this debate has deep implications for how we understand human knowledge, mental existence (wujud dhihni), and the predication of universals. The two positions lead to different ontologies of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثاني): من الأمرين اللذين ذكروهما في معنى الكلية ( أن المعلوم بها أي بالصورة العقلية (أمر كلي)، فإذا وصف الصورة بالكلية كان مجازاً على معنى أنها صورة كلي ما علم بها ( وهذا ) الأمر الثاني ( لا يليق بمن يرى العلم غير الصورة الذهنية ) المساوية في الماهية للمعلومات، بل يراه أنه صور ذهنية مخالفة لها في الماهية وتوضيح الكلام أنَّ القائلين بالصور فرقتان : فرقة تدعي أن تلك الصور مساوية في الماهية للأمور المعلومة بها، بل الصور هي ماهيات المعلومات من حيث أنها حاصلة في النفس، فيكون العلم والمعلوم متحدين بالذات مختلفين بالاعتبار كما مر وعلى قول هؤلاء يكون للأشياء وجودان وجود خارجي ووجود ذهني، وتكون الكلية عارضة للصور العقلية حقيقة، لأنها ماهيات المعلومات المحمولة على أفرادها وفرقة تزعم أن الصور العقلية مثل وأشباح للأمور المعلومة بها مخالفة لها في الماهية وعلى قولهم لا يكون للأشياء قوله : ( قيست إلى حصصها فعلى هذا يكون وصف غير الأنواع الحقيقية بالكلية بالقياس إلى أفرادها على سبيل التجوز باعتبار اشتمال تلك الأفراد على الحصص. :قوله : ( عارضة للصور العقلية أي ماهيات المعلومات من حيث حصولها في النفس حصولاً ظلياً . قوله: (قيست إلى حصصها هذا التوجيه يستدعي أن تكون كلية تلك الكليات بالقياس إلى الحصص لا غير. المستفاد قوله : ( بمن يرى العلم غير الصور الذهنية يدخل في عمومه من يرى العلم نفس التعلق أو أمراً عدمياً، وليس هذا مراداً هاهنا فلذلك قيده رحمه الله تعالى بقوله : المساوية إلخ ليكون النفي من غير راجعاً إلى القيد قوله : (بل الصور هي ماهيات المعلومات (الخ قالوا : إذا حصلت الصور في الذهن انكشفت بنفسها وصارت معلومة بذاتها لا يعلم زائد عليها وصورة منتزعة منها وإلا لتسلسلت العلوم وبتوسط هذه الصورة ينكشف الأمر الخارجي فالعلم والمعلوم الأول متحدان بالذات، وهذا حاصل ما ذكره الفارابي في تعاليقه من أن المدرك بالحقيقة هو نفس الصورة المنتقشة في وأما الشيء الذي هذه الصورة صورته فهو معلوم بالعرض فالمعلوم هو العلم وإلا كان يتسلسل إلى ما لا نهاية له إلى هاهنا عبارته . ذهنك
(The second) of the two things they mentioned in the meaning of universality (kulliyyah): (that what is known by it, i.e., by the mental form (al-surah al-'aqliyyah), is a universal (amr kulli). So when the form is described as universal, it is metaphorical in the sense that it is a form of a universal that is known through it. And (this) second thing (is not fitting for one who sees knowledge as something other than the mental form (al-surah al-dhihniyyah) that is equal in essence (musawiyah fi al-mahiyyah) to the known things, but rather sees it as mental forms that are different in essence from them. And clarification: Those who affirm forms are two groups: A group that claims that those forms are equal in essence to the known things; rather, the forms are the quiddities (mahiyyat) of the known things in terms of their being present in the soul. Thus knowledge and the known are identical in essence and different in aspect, as has passed. And according to this group, things have two existences: external existence (wujud khariji) and mental existence (wujud dhihni), and universality truly attaches to mental forms because they are the quiddities of the known things that are predicated of their individuals. And a group that claims that mental forms are like likenesses and shadows (ashbah) of the known things, different in essence from them. And according to them, things do not have... His saying: (measured against their portions): So on this basis, describing non-real species as universal in relation to their individuals is metaphorical (tajawwuz), considering that those individuals contain the portions. His saying: (attaches to mental forms) i.e., the quiddities of the known things in terms of their obtaining in the soul as a shadowy obtaining. His saying: (measured against their portions) this orientation requires that the universality of those universals is in relation to the portions only. The benefit: His saying: (for one who sees knowledge as other than mental forms) includes in its generality one who sees knowledge as the very relation (ta'alliq) or as a non-existent (amr 'adami), but this is not intended here. Therefore, the author (may God have mercy on him) restricted it by his saying: 'equal [in essence]' so that the negation would be from others returning to the restriction. His saying: (Rather, the forms are the quiddities of the known things, etc.) They said: When the forms obtain in the mind, they become disclosed by themselves and become known by themselves, without knowledge additional to them and a form derived from them; otherwise there would be an infinite regress of sciences. And through this form, the external thing becomes disclosed. So knowledge and the first known are identical in essence. This is the gist of what al-Farabi mentioned in his Ta'liqat: that the perceiver in reality is the very form imprinted in your mind, while the thing of which this form is a form is known by accident. So the known is knowledge, otherwise there would be an infinite regress. Up to here is his phrase.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage discusses the second aspect of universality (kulliyyah): that what is known by the mental form (al-surah al-'aqliyyah) is a universal. Calling the form itself universal is thus metaphorical, meaning it is a form of a universal. This second point, however, is not suitable for those who hold that knowledge is not the same as the mental form that is equal in essence to the known objects; rather, they see knowledge as something else. The passage then distinguishes two groups regarding mental forms. The first group holds that mental forms are identical in essence to the known things; knowledge and known are essentially one. For them, things have two existences: external and mental, and universality truly attaches to mental forms because they are the quiddities (mahiyyat) of the known things predicated of individuals. The second group holds that mental forms are mere likenesses and shadows (ashbah) of the known things, different in essence. The passage then includes glosses: one clarifies that describing non-real species as universal is metaphorical; another explains that 'attaches to mental forms' means the quiddities' shadowy existence in the soul; and a third gloss notes that the author's restriction to 'equal in essence' excludes other views of knowledge (like relation or non-existence). Finally, a long gloss quotes al-Farabi: the true object of perception is the form imprinted in the mind; the external thing is known by accident; otherwise infinite regress follows.

المعلوم
الصورة العقلية
الكلية الحقيقية
المساواة في الماهية
المخالفة في الماهية
الوجود الذهني
الوجود الخارجي
الفارابي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Universality is an inherent property of the mental form itself.
Correction: The passage explains that universality truly belongs to what is known through the form (the quiddity) and only metaphorically to the form, unless the form is identical to the quiddity.

Checkpoint
Why is the second aspect of universality (that the known is universal) not suitable for those who hold that knowledge is not identical to the mental form?
A) Because those thinkers deny mental forms altogether
B) Because they believe mental forms are particular and cannot represent universals
C) Because they view mental forms as mere likenesses differing in essence, so describing the form as universal is metaphorical and not appropriate
D) Because they hold that knowledge is an attribute of the soul

Key Takeaway
The reality of universality in mental forms depends on whether those forms are identical in essence to the known objects or mere likenesses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you gain knowledge about something—like a concept in this lesson—do you assume your mental picture is exactly what is real, or could it be just a shadow? How does your answer affect the confidence you place in your own understanding?
?
Al-Farabi argued that the true object of knowledge is the mental form itself, and the external thing is known only indirectly. If that is true, how can you ever be certain that your mental forms correspond to external reality? What would it take for you to verify that correspondence?
?
The passage presents two opposing views on mental forms. Which of these two groups do you find yourself instinctively leaning toward when you learn something new? Are you willing to examine that assumption today, even if it unsettles your sense of certainty?

The Unity of Knowledge and Known: Sanusi's Critique
Critique of the claim that universality belongs to the known object, arguing for the unity of knowledge and known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that the mental form and the known object are essentially one, and why universality must belong to the mental form rather than to an external object.

Core Idea
The mental form and the known are one—universality cannot reside in the external object.

Sanusi now presents his critical argument against the second faction's view that the universal is the known object itself, not the mental form. He begins with a striking example: we can conceive of pure non-existence, which has no external reality. When we know it, a relation forms between knower and known, but a relation requires two distinct things. Since the known is not external, it must be mental—thus the mental form is the essence of the known, uniting knowledge and known. This forces the conclusion that universality belongs to the mental form, overturning the opposing claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the flow of the reasoning. Notice how Sanusi builds from the example of pure negation to his conclusion.
Focus on the logical steps without yet analyzing each word.
2
Trace the logical structure
Identify the key premises: conceiving pure non-existence, the relation that arises, the requirement of two distinct things, and the location of the known.
Consider why the example of pure negation is chosen—it forces the known to be mental.
3
Grasp the conclusion and its significance
Understand why the unity of knowledge and known means universality must belong to the mental form. Reflect on how this refutes the second faction's position.
The conclusion is that the form itself is the known, so universality cannot be attributed to something external.

Source Text
