How can this principle protect you from arrogance and encourage you to seek knowledge with humility?
?
The commentator uses the principle of particularity to defend a position against an objection. When you are confronted with a religious argument, do you consider the particularities that might make the objection invalid? Or do you accept it at face value? How does this passage teach you to think more critically and avoid hasty conclusions in your own faith?

The Permission Argument: Dependence of Necessary Knowledge on Theoretical Knowledge
Presents the permission argument with the example of two opposites

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of the impossibility of two opposites coexisting is used to argue that some necessary knowledge depends on theoretical proof.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge can rest on theoretical foundations.

This lesson examines a key argument in Islamic theology about the relationship between necessary and speculative knowledge. The example of the impossibility of two opposites coexisting shows that some judgments we consider self-evident actually depend on prior theoretical reasoning. The passage presents the argument and the response, noting that the dispute is often verbal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Notice the structure: claim, example, clarification, response.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the statement that some scholars permit necessary knowledge to be based on theoretical knowledge. Understand what is being argued.
The example is the impossibility of two opposites coexisting.
3
Understand the supporting reasoning
Notice why the example is chosen: knowledge of the impossibility depends on knowledge of the existence of the two opposites. But knowledge of existence of accidents is theoretical, not necessary.
Thus necessary knowledge (impossibility) depends on theoretical (existence).
4
Examine the response
Read the commentary's remarks about the dispute being verbal. Understand that the issue may be about definitions or terminology.
The commentator favors the view that the dispute is merely verbal.
5
Reflect on the takeaway
Consider how this argument fits into broader discussions in kalam about the nature of certainty and the role of proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوزه بعضهم لأن العلم بامتناع اجتماع الضدين ذلك مبني على وجودهما والعلم به أي بوجودهما ليس ضرورياً لأن التضاد لا يكون إلا بين الأعراض والعلم بوجود الأعراض ليس بضروري ولذلك يثبت وجود الأعراض بالدليل قوله : ( فإن العلم بامتناع اجتماع إلخ ) أي التصديق بأنَّ اجتماع الضدين ممتنع لا المفهوم التصوري يدل عليه سياق الاستدلال والجواب قوله : ( مبني على وجودهما لأن الاجتماع لا يتحصل إلا بعد وجود المثبتين وإذ ليس في الذهن لعدم الوجود الذهني فهو في الخارج من عرفه ضرورة فهو مكلف به حتى يلزم قبح التكليف وقد أشرنا في المقصد الرابع في أحكام النظر إلى جوابه فلينظر فيه قوله : ( وجوزه بعضهم وهو المختار لما سبق من أن العلم بحقيقة النتيجة الحاصل عقيب النظر ضروري وكذا العلم بالعلم بالنتيجة ونحوها والحق أن النزاع لفظي كما ذكره قوله : ( مبني على وجودهما أي العلم بوجودهما على حذف المضاف كما يدل عليه قوله والعلم به ليس ضروريا قوله : ( والعلم به ليس ضرورياً فيه منع إذ قد سبق في المقصد الرابع من المرصد الأول من الموقف الثالث أن الضرورة كافية لنا في وجود العرض وأنه لا يقوم بنفسه الصفات المسماة بالأعراض مغايرة للذوات فمن لا يعلم وجود الأضداد كالسواد والبياض بذلك الدليل لم يحكم بامتناع الاجتماع بينها فقد استناد الضروري إلى النظري
And some permitted it, because knowledge of the impossibility of the coexistence of two opposites is based on their existence, and knowledge of their existence is not necessary, because opposition only occurs between accidents, and knowledge of the existence of accidents is not necessary; therefore the existence of accidents is established by proof. His statement: 'knowledge of the impossibility of coexistence' refers to the belief that the coexistence of opposites is impossible, not the conceptual notion, as indicated by the context of the argument and the response. His statement: 'based on their existence' because coexistence only occurs after the existence of the two things, and since it is not in the mind due to the lack of mental existence, it is in the external. Whoever knows it necessarily is obligated by it, otherwise the obligation would be ugly. We have referred to the answer in the fourth object in the rulings on speculation. His statement: 'and some permitted it, and it is the chosen view' because what was previously said that knowledge of the true nature of the result occurring after speculation is necessary, and likewise knowledge of the knowledge of the result, etc. The truth is that the dispute is verbal as he mentioned. His statement: 'based on their existence' means knowledge of their existence, with the deletion of the genitive, as his statement indicates: 'and knowledge of it is not necessary.' His statement: 'and knowledge of it is not necessary' is subject to objection, for it was already stated in the fourth object of the first observation of the third stance that necessity is sufficient for us in the existence of the accident and that it does not subsist by itself. The attributes called accidents are different from the essences; so whoever does not know the existence of opposites like black and white by that proof does not judge the impossibility of coexistence between them; thus necessary knowledge depends on theoretical.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents the argument for those who permit the dependence of necessary knowledge on theoretical knowledge. They use the example of the knowledge that two opposites cannot coexist. This knowledge is based on the existence of the opposites and the knowledge that they exist. However, knowledge of the existence of accidents (opposites) is not necessary; it is attained through proof. Therefore, the necessary knowledge (that opposites cannot coexist) depends on theoretical knowledge (the proof of the existence of accidents). The commentator explains that the judgment here is the belief, not the concept. He also notes that the chosen view is that the dispute is verbal.

Key Terms
الضدين
الامتناع
العلم الضروري
العلم النظري
الأعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the impossibility itself is known through proof, but the argument is that the impossibility is necessary while the knowledge of existence is theoretical.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the necessary knowledge is the belief in impossibility, but it depends on the theoretical knowledge of existence.

Checkpoint
What example do the proponents use to argue that necessary knowledge can depend on theoretical knowledge?
A) Knowledge of the existence of God.
B) Knowledge of the impossibility of two opposites coexisting.
C) Knowledge of the necessity of speculation.
D) Knowledge of the creation of the world.

Key Takeaway
The permission argument shows that some necessary knowledge relies on theoretical premises, and the dispute over this is often verbal rather than substantive.

The Self-Refutation of Denying Knowledge of the Impossible
Refutes the denial argument by showing self-refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying the possibility of knowing the impossible is self-refuting and how this refutation is grounded in the nature of judgment.

Core Idea
To deny knowledge of the impossible already entails knowing it.

This passage addresses a common objection against the necessity of certain rational judgments. The opponent argues that the impossible is not a thing and thus cannot be known, but the rebuttal exposes the inconsistency in this position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
Read the passage and find the opponent's argument: that knowledge of the impossibility of opposites is impossible because the impossible is not a thing.
The opponent relies on a definition of knowledge as belief of a thing as it is.
2
Understand the rebuttal
The commentator argues that the opponent's denial itself implies knowledge. By judging that the impossibility is unknowable, the opponent already has a concept of it.
This is a self-refutation: the judgment of unknowability requires some knowledge of the object.
3
Reflect on the consequence
Realize that denying necessary truths leads to contradiction. The opponent is called 'obstinate and contradictory'.
This argument is foundational for establishing the necessity of rational judgments in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أجاب عن هذا الاستدلال بأن منع العلم به أي بامتناع اجتماع الضدين بناء على أن العلم هو اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به والمستحيل ليس بشيء فهو مكابر ومناقض لقوله فإن حكمه بعدم معلومية ذلك الامتناع يستلزم العلم به كما مر في أوائل الكتاب بل الحق في الجواب عنه أنه أي العلم بامتناع اجتماع الضدين لا يتوقف على وجودهما في الخارج، إذ لا توقف للتصديق على وجود أطرافه وأما تصورهما أي تصور الضدين فنعم قوله : ( مغايرة للذوات) أي بحسب الوجود سواء كانت عين الذات كالمقدار فإنه عين الجواهر المنتظمة أو أموراً اعتبارية كالأعراض النسبية قوله : ( ومنع العلم به أي بإنكار العلم به فضلاً عن كونه ضرورياً كما يرشد إليه دليله وقد صرح به المصنف في تعريف العلم حيث قال : ومن أنكر تعلق العلم بالمستحيل فهو مكابر ومناقض قوله : ( والمستحيل ليس بشيء فلا يتعلق به العلم به تصوراً ولا تصديقاً قوله : (مانع مقتضی عقله فإن كل عاقل يجد من نفسه الحكم باستحالة اجتماع الضدين ولا يتصور ذلك إلا مع كون اجتماعهما معلوماً بوجه ما فتعلق به العلم التصديقي والتصوري قوله : ( فإن حكمه بعدم إلخ ) كما ساق إليه دليله والتزم قوله : ( يستلزم العلم به أي تصوره إذ الحكم على المجهول المطلق محال فقد لزمه من الحكم عليه بعدم المعلومية معلوميته له تصديقاً وتصوراً فافهم فإنه قد زل فيه أقدام بعض الناظرين قوله : ( إذ لا توقف للتصديق أي لكل تصديق على وجود الأطراف بل إذا كان تصديقاً وهي قوله : ( بناء على أن العلم الخ فإن قلت : متعلق العلم فيما ذكر هو الامتناع لا المستحيل الذي هو الاجتماع، فبناء المنع على ما ذكره غير صحيح، قلت : العلم بامتناع الاجتماع لا يستلزم العلم باجتماع نفسه، وانتفاء اللازم يدل على انتفاء الملزوم على أن الامتناع نفسه مستحيل أيضاً لأنه مما يمتنع وجوده في الخارج وإلا لزم وجود موضوعه فيه قوله : ( فإن حكمه بعدم معلومية الخ) فيه مناقشة أن منع المعلومية هو المذكور فيما سبق، ومنعها ليس حكماً بعدمها وإلا كان المانع مدعياً فالأظهر في العبارة أن يقال : فإن منع معلوميته يستدعي تصوره، ويمكن أن يدفع بأن منع الشيء وإن لم يكن حكماً بعدمه مطلقاً إلا أن بناء المنع هاهنا على ما أشار إليه الشارح، بل المصنف أيضاً في صدر الكتاب يدل على أنهم يحكمون بانتفاء المعلومية، لكن يرد عليه أن مراد المانع منع التصديق بامتناع اجتماع الضدين لأنه الذي ادعى ضروريته فيما سبق، ويدل عليه قول الشارح أيضاً لم يحكم بامتناع الاجتماع فلا
And whoever responds to this argument by saying that the denial of knowledge of it, i.e., the impossibility of the coexistence of two opposites, is based on the fact that knowledge is belief of a thing as it is and the impossible is not a thing—then he is being obstinate and contradictory to his own statement, because his judgment of the unknowability of that impossibility implies knowledge of it, as mentioned earlier in the beginning of the book. Rather, the truth in response is that the knowledge of the impossibility of the coexistence of two opposites does not depend on their existence in the external, because belief does not depend on the existence of its terms; as for their conceptualization, i.e., the conceptualization of the two opposites, yes. His statement: 'different from the essences' means in terms of existence, whether they are identical to the essence like magnitude, for it is identical to the ordered substances, or are relative matters like relative accidents. His statement: 'and the denial of knowledge of it' means the denial of knowledge of it, let alone its being necessary, as his proof indicates; and the author stated in the definition of knowledge: 'Whoever denies the relation of knowledge to the impossible is being obstinate and contradictory.' His statement: 'and the impossible is not a thing, so knowledge does not pertain to it, neither conceptually nor as belief.' His statement: 'denying the requirement of his intellect' because every rational person finds in himself the judgment of the impossibility of the coexistence of two opposites, and he does not conceive that except with the fact that their coexistence is known in some way, so conceptual and belief knowledge pertains to it. His statement: 'for his judgment of being unknown' as he followed his proof and committed himself. His statement: 'entails knowledge of it' i.e., its conceptualization, because judgment about the absolutely unknown is impossible; so from his judgment about it being unknown, its knowability for him is entailed both as belief and concept. Understand, for some researchers have slipped here. His statement: 'because belief does not depend' i.e., every belief does not depend on the existence of its terms; rather, if it is a belief... His statement: 'based on the fact that knowledge, etc.' If you say: the object of knowledge in what was mentioned is the impossibility, not the impossible which is the coexistence; so basing the denial on what he mentioned is not correct. I say: knowledge of the impossibility of coexistence does not entail knowledge of coexistence itself, and the negation of the consequent indicates the negation of the antecedent; besides, impossibility itself is also impossible because it is something whose existence in the external is impossible, otherwise its subject's existence in it would be entailed. His statement: 'for his judgment of being unknown' contains a discussion: the denial of knowability is what was mentioned earlier, and its denial is not a judgment of its non-existence; otherwise the denier would be a claimant. So the more apparent in the expression is to say: for his denial of its knowability requires its conceptualization. And it can be countered by saying that the denial of a thing, though not a judgment of its non-existence absolutely, yet the basis of the denial here is as the commentator indicated; indeed, the author also in the beginning of the book indicates that they judge the negation of knowability. But it is objected that the denier intends the denial of belief in the impossibility of the coexistence of two opposites, because he claimed its necessity earlier, and the commentator's statement also indicates: 'he did not judge the impossibility of coexistence, so...'
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents the rebuttal to the permission argument. The rebuttal argues that denying that one can know the impossibility of two opposites coexisting is self-refuting because the denial itself implies some knowledge of that impossibility. The correct answer is that the necessary knowledge of impossibility does not depend on the external existence of the opposites; rather, only their conceptualization is needed. The commentator then discusses objections about whether impossibility is a thing, the nature of judgment, and the relation between belief and its terms. He emphasizes that the dispute is often verbal and that the denier's position is inconsistent.

Key Terms
المكابر
المستحيل
الامتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since the impossible does not exist externally, it cannot be known at all.
Correction: The correction is that knowledge of impossibility is possible because it does not require external existence of the impossible object; it only requires conceptualization. Moreover, denying such knowledge is self-refuting.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator consider the opponent 'obstinate and contradictory'?
A) Because the opponent denies a necessary truth.
B) Because the opponent's denial of knowledge implies knowledge of the impossibility.
C) Because the opponent violates logical rules.
D) Because the opponent fails to define knowledge.

Key Takeaway
To deny knowledge of the impossible is self-refuting because the denial itself implies a concept of the impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a truth because it didn't fit your narrow definition of what can be known? In that rejection, were you not already acknowledging the truth you denied? How will you confront that self-contradiction in your own heart today?
?
When you encounter a belief that seems impossible to you, do you dismiss it as unknowable? Consider that your dismissal itself implies you have some concept of it. What does that reveal about your intellectual humility?
?
The opponent in this lesson is called 'obstinate' for persisting in a contradictory position. Have you ever clung to a view that was self-refuting? What would it cost you to admit it, and are you willing to pay that price?

The Correct Doctrine: Knowledge of Impossibility Does Not Require External Existence
Presents the correct answer about dependence of knowledge on external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct position that knowledge (tasdiq) of the impossibility of opposites does not require the external existence of the opposites themselves, only their conceptualization.

Core Idea
Belief in impossibility needs only concepts, not external things.

Having refuted the denial, the text now presents the correct doctrine. It distinguishes between conceptualization and belief, showing that belief in impossibility depends only on conceptualizing the terms, not on their external existence.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between tasdiq and tasawwur
Read the passage and note the distinction: belief (tasdiq) does not require the external existence of its objects, but conceptualization (tasawwur) does require having concepts of the terms.
This distinction is crucial for understanding how we can know impossibilities.
2
Understand the main claim
The correct answer is that knowledge of the impossibility of two opposites coexisting does not depend on the opposites existing externally. Only the conceptualization of 'two opposites' and 'coexistence' is needed.
This preserves the possibility of necessary knowledge without requiring actual instances.
3
Consider the objection and answer
An objection is raised: the object of knowledge is the impossibility itself, not the opposites. The answer clarifies that knowledge of impossibility does not entail knowledge of the opposites' existence.
This reinforces the distinction between the concept of impossibility and the concept of the impossible.

And whoever responds to this argument by saying that the denial of knowledge of it, i.e., the impossibility of the coexistence of two opposites, is based on the fact that knowledge is belief of a thing as it is and the impossible is not a thing—then he is being obstinate and contradictory to his own statement, because his judgment of the unknowability of that impossibility implies knowledge of it, as mentioned earlier in the beginning of the book. Rather, the truth in response is that the knowledge of the impossibility of the coexistence of two opposites does not depend on their existence in the external, because belief does not depend on the existence of its terms; as for their conceptualization, i.e., the conceptualization of the two opposites, yes. His statement: 'different from the essences' means in terms of existence, whether they are identical to the essence like magnitude, for it is identical to the ordered substances, or are relative matters like relative accidents. His statement: 'and the denial of knowledge of it' means the denial of knowledge of it, let alone its being necessary, as his proof indicates; and the author stated in the definition of knowledge: 'Whoever denies the relation of knowledge to the impossible is being obstinate and contradictory.' His statement: 'and the impossible is not a thing, so knowledge does not pertain to it, neither conceptually nor as belief.' His statement: 'denying the requirement of his intellect' because every rational person finds in himself the judgment of the impossibility of the coexistence of two opposites, and he does not conceive that except with the fact that their coexistence is known in some way, so conceptual and belief knowledge pertains to it. His statement: 'for his judgment of being unknown' as he followed his proof and committed himself. His statement: 'entails knowledge of it' i.e., its conceptualization, because judgment about the absolutely unknown is impossible; so from his judgment about it being unknown, its knowability for him is entailed both as belief and concept. Understand, for some researchers have slipped here. His statement: 'because belief does not depend' i.e., every belief does not depend on the existence of its terms; rather, if it is a belief... His statement: 'based on the fact that knowledge, etc.' If you say: the object of knowledge in what was mentioned is the impossibility, not the impossible which is the coexistence; so basing the denial on what he mentioned is not correct. I say: knowledge of the impossibility of coexistence does not entail knowledge of coexistence itself, and the negation of the consequent indicates the negation of the antecedent; besides, impossibility itself is also impossible because it is something whose existence in the external is impossible, otherwise its subject's existence in it would be entailed. His statement: 'for his judgment of being unknown' contains a discussion: the denial of knowability is what was mentioned earlier, and its denial is not a judgment of its non-existence; otherwise the denier would be a claimant. So the more apparent in the expression is to say: for his denial of its knowability requires its conceptualization. And it can be countered by saying that the denial of a thing, though not a judgment of its non-existence absolutely, yet the basis of the denial here is as the commentator indicated; indeed, the author also in the beginning of the book indicates that they judge the negation of knowability. But it is objected that the denier intends the denial of belief in the impossibility of the coexistence of two opposites, because he claimed its necessity earlier, and the commentator's statement also indicates: 'he did not judge the impossibility of coexistence, so...'
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents the rebuttal to the permission argument. The rebuttal argues that denying that one can know the impossibility of two opposites coexisting is self-refuting because the denial itself implies some knowledge of that impossibility. The correct answer is that the necessary knowledge of impossibility does not depend on the external existence of the opposites; rather, only their conceptualization is needed. The commentator then discusses objections about whether impossibility is a thing, the nature of judgment, and the relation between belief and its terms. He emphasizes that the dispute is often verbal and that the denier's position is inconsistent.

Correction: Correction: knowledge of impossibility only requires conceptualizing the terms, not the external existence of the impossible thing.

Two Ways to Conceive the Impossible: Analogy and Negation
Sanusi enumerates the two methods for conceiving the impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct methods by which the mind can conceive something impossible: analogy (tashbīh) and negation (nafy).

Core Idea
The impossible is conceived not by direct form but through analogy or negation.

In theological debates, we often need to conceive of impossibilities. But how can we conceive something that has no possible existence? This passage presents the two primary methods: analogy (tashbīh) and negation (nafy), showing that we do not directly picture the impossible but approach it indirectly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice that it begins with a classification of two methods.
The passage is from Sanusi’s commentary on Ibn Sina.
2
Identify the two methods
Find the phrases 'on the way of analogy' (على سبيل التشبيه) and 'on the way of negation' (على سبيل النفي). Understand how each method works using the example of blackness and sweetness.
3
Reflect on the difference
Ask yourself: In which method do we have a mental image of something actual? In which do we only have a negation?
Analogy uses an actual combination; negation directly denies existence.

النفي بأن يعقل أنه لا يمكن أن يوجد مفهوم هو اجتماع السواد والبياض فقد يعقل هاهنا المستحيل المخصوص. في تفصيل فلا يمكن أن يتصور النسبة إلا بنوع من المقابلة بالوجود وإلا بالنسبة إليه كقولنا : الخلاء وضده السواد والبياض، إذ لا يمكن أخذه معدولة وإلا اقتضى وجود المثل لما تعقلنا بخصوصه في الخارج أو في الذهن أعني الاجتماع المستحيل، وأن خلاصة ما ذكره بقوله وأما على سبيل النفي أنه يعقل بوجه عام ثم يحكم عليه النفي، ولا شك أن في الصورة النسبية آلة الملاحظة والحكم هو القدر المشترك، فلا فرق بين الوجهين، وغاية التوجيه أن بيان تصوره بوجه عام بطريقين.
Its conception, i.e., conception of the impossible, is either by way of analogy, such as conceiving between blackness and sweetness something which is combination, then it is said: 'Such a thing that we conceived between blackness and sweetness cannot occur between blackness and whiteness.' Thus the combination between blackness and sweetness is a conceived, intelligible thing of which a form in itself has occurred in the mind, unlike the combination between blackness and whiteness, since there occurred in the mind only a form by way of comparison and analogy. Or by way of negation, such as conceiving that there cannot exist a concept which is the combination of blackness and whiteness; thus here the specific impossible is conceived. He said: 'Its conception etc.' in a section from the first article of the fifth of the Logic of the Shifa', and here there is a point of doubt: How can the non-existent essence whose existence is impossible be conceived when asked about it as it is, so that afterwards it is asked whether it is? For if no meaning of it has occurred in the mind, how can it be conceived as existent or non-existent? And the impossible has no form in existence, so how can a form of it occur in the mind such that the conceived thing is its meaning? So we say in answer: This impossible, either it is simple with no composition in it and no... by way of analogy... or by way of negation... in detail: the relation cannot be conceived except by a kind of comparison to existence or in relation to it, like our saying: 'void' and its opposite—for void is conceived as being for bodies like the opposite, and its opposite-ness is conceived as being for God, just as for hot and cold. Thus the impossible is conceived by a form of a possible thing to which the impossible is related and its relation to it is conceived. As for its essence, it is not conceived or intelligible and has no essence. As for that which has composition and detail, like 'anqa' and a human that flies, it is conceived first by its details which are [possible] then the conjunction of those details is conceived on the model of the conjunction found in the details of described composite beings. Thus there are three things: two of them are parts, each of which exists, and the third is the composition between them, and it is conceived in terms of composition because composition in terms of what it is is existent. In this way, the name of the non-existent gives indication, so the non-existent is prior to existents. End. And its summary is that the impossible is not judged by a positive judgment, nor does it have a specific existence in the external or in the mind; rather it is apprehended by relative judgments that necessitate its conception in some way. This is achieved by the prior conception of beings similar to it and beings that are parts of it. And by what we have mentioned, what was supposed from the proof of mental existence is refuted—that it would require that the impossible has a specific existence in the mind. And also what is in Sharh al-Maqasid that what Ibn Sina mentioned is an admission that knowledge of the impossible is not the form. Then know that the disjunction mentioned by the author and followed by the commentator is not in the words of the Master (Ibn Sina) at all, even though it is objected that the form of analogy also is a judgment by way of negation, as indicated by the author's statement: 'Such a thing that we conceived cannot occur between blackness and whiteness'—for it cannot be taken as negated, otherwise it would require the existence of the likeness of what we conceived specifically in the external or in the mind, namely the impossible combination. And that the summary of what he mentioned by his saying 'And by way of negation' is that it is conceived in a general manner, then negation is judged upon it. And there is no doubt that in the relative form, the instrument of observation and judgment is the common denominator, so there is no difference between the two aspects. And the ultimate guidance is that its conception in a general way is by two methods.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of how the impossible can be conceived despite lacking a mental form. It presents two methods: analogy and negation. First, by analogy: we conceive an actual relation (e.g., combination between blackness and sweetness) and then deny that this relation can hold between the impossible pair. Thus, we have a mental form of the actual relation and use it to indicate the impossible. Second, by negation: we directly conceive the impossibility of a certain concept existing. Then a doubt is raised: how can we conceive something that has no meaning at all? The answer is that the impossible is either simple (without composition) or composite. For simple impossibilities, we conceive them only through their relation to existents, like 'void' is conceived as relative to bodies. For composite impossibilities (e.g., a flying human), we conceive the parts individually (which are possible) and then combine them in a way that is not actually realized. Thus, the impossible is conceived through prior concepts of existents. The passage then notes that this resolves the worry that the proof of mental existence would force the impossible to have a specific mental form. It also clarifies that Ibn Sina himself did not use this disjunction, but the author's classification helps explain the general conception.

نفي
مستحيل
تصور
مقايسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossible must have a specific mental form to be conceived.
Correction: The passage shows that the impossible is conceived either by analogy (using a possible form) or by negation, not by a direct form of the impossible itself.

Checkpoint

B) Only by negation

Key Takeaway
The mind can conceive the impossible only indirectly, through analogy to what is possible or through negation.

The Doubt and Resolution for Simple Impossibles
Sanusi resolves a doubt about conceiving the impossible through relation to existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the doubt about how the impossible can be conceived and how simple impossibles are conceived through relation to existents.

Core Idea
The impossible is conceived through relation to existents, not by a mental form of itself.

The previous lesson introduced two methods, but a deeper doubt arises: how can we conceive something that has no meaning in the mind at all? This passage presents that doubt and provides a detailed answer for simple (non-composite) impossibles, showing that they are conceived through their relation to existing things.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt
Read the doubt carefully: if the impossible has no form in existence, how can it have a form in the mind?
This is a common objection in kalam.
2
Understand the answer for simple impossibles
The answer distinguishes between simple and composite. Focus on the simple case: the impossible is conceived through its relation to existents (e.g., void conceived relative to bodies).
3
Grasp the role of relative judgments
Notice that the passage says the impossible is conceived 'by a form of a possible thing to which it is related.' This highlights that our conception is indirect.
Relative judgments are key in kalam epistemology.

Source Text

فتصوره أي تصور المستحيل إما على سبيل التشبيه بأن يعقل مثلا بين السواد والحلاوة أمر هو الاجتماع ثم يقال : مثل هذا الأمر الذي تعقلناه بين السواد والحلاوة لا يمكن حصوله بين السواد والبياض فالاجتماع بين السواد والحلاوة متصور معقول قد حصل منه في نفسه صورة في العقل بخلاف الاجتماع بين السواد والبياض؛ إذ لم يحصل منه في العقل إلا صورة بطريق المقايسة والتشبيه وأما على سبيل النفي بأن يعقل أنه لا يمكن أن يوجد مفهوم هو اجتماع السواد والبياض فقد يعقل هاهنا المستحيل المخصوص. قوله: فتصوره إلخ في فصل من المقالة الأولى من الخامس من منطق الشفاء، وهاهنا موضع شك في أن المعدوم الذات المحال الوجود كيف يتصور إذا سئل عنه بما هو حتى يطلب بعد ذلك، هل هو فإنه إن لم يحصل له في العقل معنى كيف يتصور بأنه حاصل أو غير حاصل والمحال لا صورة له في الوجود، فكيف يوجد عنه صورة في الذهن يكون ذلك المتصور معناه ؟ فنقول في جوابه : إن هذا المحال إما أن يكون مفرداً لا تركيب فيه ولا على سبيل التشبيه بأن يعقل مثلا بين السواد والحلاوة أمر هو الاجتماع ثم يقال : مثل هذا الأمر الذي تعقلناه بين السواد والحلاوة لا يمكن حصوله بين السواد والبياض فالاجتماع بين السواد والحلاوة متصور معقول قد حصل منه في نفسه صورة في العقل بخلاف الاجتماع بين السواد والبياض؛ إذ لم يحصل منه في العقل إلا صورة بطريق المقايسة والتشبيه وأما على سبيل النفي بأن يعقل أنه لا يمكن أن يوجد مفهوم هو اجتماع السواد والبياض فقد يعقل هاهنا المستحيل المخصوص. في تفصيل فلا يمكن أن يتصور النسبة إلا بنوع من المقابلة بالوجود وإلا بالنسبة إليه كقولنا : الخلاء وضده فإن الخلاء يتصور بأنه للأجسام كالمقابل وضديته يتصور بأن الله تعالى، كما للحار البارد، فيكون المحال متصوراً بصورة أمر ممكناً ينسب إليه المحال ويتصور نسبته إليها، وأما ذاته، فلا متصور أو لا معقولاً ولا ذات له، وأما الذي فيه تركيب وتفصيل مثل عنقاء وإنسان ما يطير فإنما يتصور أولا تفاصيله التي محاله ثم يتضور لتلك التفاصيل اقترانه على قياس الاقتران الموجود في تفاصيل الأشياء الموصوفة المركبة ،الذوات فيكون هناك أشياء ثلاثة : اثنان منها جزآن كل ما يقرؤه ،موجود والثالث تأليف بينهما وهو من جهة تأليف متصور بسبب أن التأليف من جهة ما هو موجود فعلى هذا النحو يعطي دلالة اسم المعدوم، فيكون المعدوم إنما متقدماً للموجودات .انتهى وخلاصته أن المستحيل لا يحكم عليه بحكم ثبوتي حتى وجوداً بخصوصه ما في الخارج أو في الذهن بأحكام نسبية تستدعي تعقله بوجه ما، وذلك حاصل لتقدم تصور الموجودات المشابهة له والموجودات التي هي أجزاء له وبما ذكرنا اندفع ما توهم من أنه لو تم دليل الوجود الذهني يوجب أن يكون للمستحيل بخصوصه وجود في العقل ، وما في شرح المقاصد من أن ما ذكر ابن سينا اعتراف بأن العلم بالمستحيل ليس الصورة ثم اعلم أن الترديد الذي ذكره المصنف وتبعه الشارح، ليس في كلام الشيخ أصلا، مع أنه يرد أن صورة التشبيه أيضاً الحكم على سبيل النفي كما يدل عليه قول المصنف مثل هذا الأمر الذي تعقلناه ولا يمكن حصوله بين السواد والبياض، إذ لا يمكن أخذه معدولة وإلا اقتضى وجود المثل لما تعقلنا بخصوصه في الخارج أو في الذهن أعني الاجتماع المستحيل، وأن خلاصة ما ذكره بقوله وأما على سبيل النفي أنه يعقل بوجه عام ثم يحكم عليه النفي، ولا شك أن في الصورة النسبية آلة الملاحظة والحكم هو القدر المشترك، فلا فرق بين الوجهين، وغاية التوجيه أن بيان تصوره بوجه عام بطريقين.
Its conception, i.e., conception of the impossible, is either by way of analogy, such as conceiving between blackness and sweetness something which is combination, then it is said: 'Such a thing that we conceived between blackness and sweetness cannot occur between blackness and whiteness.' Thus the combination between blackness and sweetness is a conceived, intelligible thing of which a form in itself has occurred in the mind, unlike the combination between blackness and whiteness, since there occurred in the mind only a form by way of comparison and analogy. Or by way of negation, such as conceiving that there cannot exist a concept which is the combination of blackness and whiteness; thus here the specific impossible is conceived. He said: 'Its conception etc.' in a section from the first article of the fifth of the Logic of the Shifa', and here there is a point of doubt: How can the non-existent essence whose existence is impossible be conceived when asked about it as it is, so that afterwards it is asked whether it is? For if no meaning of it has occurred in the mind, how can it be conceived as existent or non-existent? And the impossible has no form in existence, so how can a form of it occur in the mind such that the conceived thing is its meaning? So we say in answer: This impossible, either it is simple with no composition in it and no... by way of analogy... or by way of negation... in detail: the relation cannot be conceived except by a kind of comparison to existence or in relation to it, like our saying: 'void' and its opposite—for void is conceived as being for bodies like the opposite, and its opposite-ness is conceived as being for God, just as for hot and cold. Thus the impossible is conceived by a form of a possible thing to which the impossible is related and its relation to it is conceived. As for its essence, it is not conceived or intelligible and has no essence. As for that which has composition and detail, like 'anqa' and a human that flies, it is conceived first by its details which are [possible] then the conjunction of those details is conceived on the model of the conjunction found in the details of described composite beings. Thus there are three things: two of them are parts, each of which exists, and the third is the composition between them, and it is conceived in terms of composition because composition in terms of what it is is existent. In this way, the name of the non-existent gives indication, so the non-existent is prior to existents. End. And its summary is that the impossible is not judged by a positive judgment, nor does it have a specific existence in the external or in the mind; rather it is apprehended by relative judgments that necessitate its conception in some way. This is achieved by the prior conception of beings similar to it and beings that are parts of it. And by what we have mentioned, what was supposed from the proof of mental existence is refuted—that it would require that the impossible has a specific existence in the mind. And also what is in Sharh al-Maqasid that what Ibn Sina mentioned is an admission that knowledge of the impossible is not the form. Then know that the disjunction mentioned by the author and followed by the commentator is not in the words of the Master (Ibn Sina) at all, even though it is objected that the form of analogy also is a judgment by way of negation, as indicated by the author's statement: 'Such a thing that we conceived cannot occur between blackness and whiteness'—for it cannot be taken as negated, otherwise it would require the existence of the likeness of what we conceived specifically in the external or in the mind, namely the impossible combination. And that the summary of what he mentioned by his saying 'And by way of negation' is that it is conceived in a general manner, then negation is judged upon it. And there is no doubt that in the relative form, the instrument of observation and judgment is the common denominator, so there is no difference between the two aspects. And the ultimate guidance is that its conception in a general way is by two methods.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of how the impossible can be conceived despite lacking a mental form. It presents two methods: analogy and negation. First, by analogy: we conceive an actual relation (e.g., combination between blackness and sweetness) and then deny that this relation can hold between the impossible pair. Thus, we have a mental form of the actual relation and use it to indicate the impossible. Second, by negation: we directly conceive the impossibility of a certain concept existing. Then a doubt is raised: how can we conceive something that has no meaning at all? The answer is that the impossible is either simple (without composition) or composite. For simple impossibilities, we conceive them only through their relation to existents, like 'void' is conceived as relative to bodies. For composite impossibilities (e.g., a flying human), we conceive the parts individually (which are possible) and then combine them in a way that is not actually realized. Thus, the impossible is conceived through prior concepts of existents. The passage then notes that this resolves the worry that the proof of mental existence would force the impossible to have a specific mental form. It also clarifies that Ibn Sina himself did not use this disjunction, but the author's classification helps explain the general conception.

مستحيل
تصور
خلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossible can be directly imagined in the mind as a positive form.
Correction: The passage argues that the impossible has no direct form; it is only conceived through relation or negation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is a simple impossible like 'void' conceived?
A) Through a direct form in the mind
B) Through its relation to bodies
C) By imagining a picture
D) It cannot be conceived

Key Takeaway
Simple impossibles are conceived only by their relation to existents, not by a positive form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider something that is impossible for Allah—like a partner or a son—are you merely negating a concept, or are you truly conceiving something? How does the lesson's claim that 'the impossible itself has no essence' change the way you think about tawheed? Commit to reminding yourself that your understanding of Allah's oneness is not built on conceiving its opposite, but on affirming His perfection alone.
?
You routinely imagine impossible scenarios like 'void' or 'nothingness,' but this lesson says those have no true essence in the mind. What does that reveal about the limits of your own intellect? Will you humbly acknowledge that much of what you think you 'know' is only relational, and turn your attention to what can be truly conceived of Allah through His attributes?
?
If every impossible thing can only be conceived through its relation to possible existents, then your ability to understand even the simplest impossibles depends on what actually exists. How does that dependency on creation affect your reliance on Allah as the only truly Necessary Being? Pledge to evaluate your thoughts: are you using creation as a crutch to think about the Creator, or are you letting revelation guide your conception of Him?

Composite Impossibles and the Conclusion
Sanusi explains composite impossibles and concludes with a summary and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how composite impossibles are conceived through their parts and how the overall argument refutes the need for mental existence of the impossible.

Core Idea
Composite impossibles are conceived by combining possible parts in an impossible way.

After addressing simple impossibles, the passage turns to composite ones, such as a flying human. These are conceived by first imagining the individual possible parts and then combining them, even though the combination is impossible. The passage then draws a conclusion about the nature of conception of the impossible and addresses a potential objection about mental existence.

Guided Path
1
Read about composite impossibles
Read the section on composite impossibles (e.g., 'anqa' and flying human). Notice how the parts are conceived individually before combination.
2
Identify the three elements
The passage mentions three things: two parts (each existing) and the composition between them (which is also conceived as existent).
This shows that composite impossibles rely on existent parts.
3
Understand the final summary
The summary concludes that the impossible is judged by relative judgments, not positive ones. It also refutes the objection from mental existence.
This wraps up the entire discussion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been preoccupied with a fear or desire that you know is impossible—like reliving a past sin or imagining a perfect worldly life? ?

?
You conceive of composite impossibles by combining known parts. Consider the spiritual danger of combining half-truths to form false beliefs about Allah or yourself. What 'composite' of your own ideas might be leading you away from sound aqeedah? How will you dismantle it?

The Nature of Will: Mu'tazili vs Ash'ari
Sharh al-Mawaqif defines irada and presents two opposing definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two competing definitions of will (irada) in kalam—the Mu'tazili view as belief in benefit and the Ash'ari view as an inclination following belief—and the key arguments and counterarguments for each.

Core Idea
Will is not just belief in benefit; it is the inclination that follows such belief.

The passage introduces the third type of psychical qualities: will (irada). It presents two opposing definitions: the Mu'tazili position that will is belief in benefit or its estimation, and the Ash'ari position that will is an inclination (mayl) that follows such belief. The Ash'ari support their view with introspective evidence and a counterexample, while the Mu'tazila refine their definition to address the objection. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each view.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overall sense of the debate.
Notice the structure: definition, Mu'tazili view, Ash'ari view, objection, response.
2
Distinguish the two definitions
Identify the Mu'tazili definition (irada = belief in benefit) and the Ash'ari definition (irada = inclination following belief). Note the terms 'dā'iya' and 'mayl'.
The Ash'ari argue the two are distinct; the Mu'tazila conflate them.
3
Examine the Ash'ari proofs
Look at the two arguments: (1) we experience a distinct inclination after belief, and (2) we often believe in benefit but do not intend until inclination arises.
These aim to show that belief alone is insufficient for will.
4
Analyze the Mu'tazili response
The Mu'tazila refine their definition: will is belief in benefit for oneself or a cared-for other without impediment. They argue that the inclination only occurs when the person is not truly capable.
Consider whether this response successfully counteracts the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث من أنواع الكيفيات النفسانية الإرادة وفيها أي في الإرادة وفي بعض النسخ وفيه أي في هذا النوع مقاصد سبعة. المقصد الأول في تعريفها قيل إنها أي الإرادة اعتقاد النفع أو ظنه، والقائل بذلك كثير من المعتزلة قالوا إن نسبة القدرة إلى طرفي الفعل على السوية، فإذا حصل اعتقاد النفع أو ظنه في أحد طرفيه ترجح على الآخر عند القادر وأثر فيه قدرته وقيل ليس الإرادة ما ذكر من الاعتقاد أو الظن بل هذا هو المسمى بالداعية، وأما الإرادة فهي ميل يتبع ذلك الاعتقاد أو الظن كما أن الكراهة نفرة تتبع اعتقاد الضرر أو ظنه، وليست الإرادة من قبيل الاعتقاد والظن فإنا نجد من أنفسنا بعد اعتقاد أن الفعل الفلاني فيه جلب نفع أو دفع ضر ميلاً إليه مترتباً على ذلك الاعتقاد، وهو أي الميل الذي نجده أمر مغاير للعلم بالنفع أو دفع الضر ضرورة لا شبهة فيها، وأيضاً فإن القادر كثيراً ما يعتقد النفع في فعل أو يظنه ومع ذلك لا يريده ما لم يحصل له هذا الميل، وقد أجيب عن ذلك بأنا لا ندعي أن الإرادة اعتقاد النفع أو ظنه مطلقاً، بل نقول هي اعتقاد نفع له أو لغيره ممن يؤثر خيره بحيث يمكن وصوله إلى أحدهما بلا ممانعة مانع من تعب أو معارضة والميل الذي ذكرتموه إنما يحصل لمن لا يقدر كذلك لا يكون الاعتقاد المذكور مرجحاً لأحد الطرفين فلا يكون إرادة.
The third type of psychical qualities: will, and in it—i.e., concerning will—and in some copies 'concerning it,' i.e., concerning this type, there are seven purposes. The first purpose: its definition. It is said that it—i.e., will—is belief in benefit or its estimation, and the proponents of that are many of the Mu'tazila, who said: The relation of power to the two sides of the act is equal; when belief in benefit or its estimation occurs on one of its two sides, it preponderates over the other with the capable agent and his power acts upon it. And it is said: Will is not what was mentioned of belief or estimation; rather that is what is called 'al-dā'iya' (motive). As for will, it is an inclination that follows that belief or estimation, just as aversion is a repulsion that follows belief in harm or its estimation. And will is not of the genus of belief and estimation, for we find in ourselves after believing that a particular act involves obtaining benefit or repelling harm, an inclination toward it consequent upon that belief; and it—i.e., the inclination we find—is a thing distinct from knowledge of benefit or repulsion of harm, by necessity with no doubt about it. Also, the capable agent often believes in the benefit of an act or estimates it and yet does not intend it until this inclination occurs to him. And it was answered to that: We do not claim that will is belief in benefit or its estimation absolutely; rather we say: It is belief in benefit for oneself or for another whom one cares about in such a way that it is possible for benefit to reach one of them without prevention by any obstacle of trouble or opposition. The inclination you mention only occurs for one who is not capable, thus the mentioned belief is not a preponderator for one of the two sides, so it is not will.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of will (irada) within the third type of psychical qualities. It begins by introducing the seven purposes (maqāṣid) concerning will, then focuses on the first purpose: definition. It presents the Mu'tazili view that will is belief in benefit or its estimation, based on the principle that power (qudra) is neutral with respect to the two sides of an act, and belief in benefit tips the scale. Then it presents the opposing Ash'ari view that will is not belief but a consequent inclination (mayl) following that belief, analogous to aversion (karāha) following belief in harm. The Ash'ari support their view by observing that we experience a distinct inclination after belief, which is separate from the belief itself, and that one often believes in benefit but does not act until that inclination arises. The Mu'tazila respond by refining their definition: will is belief in benefit for oneself or for another whom one cares about, provided there is no impediment; they argue that the inclination mentioned only occurs for those who are not truly capable, and thus belief serves as the preponderator. The passage thus lays out the core disagreement on the nature of irada.

Key Terms
إرادة
اعتقاد النفع
ميل
داعية
كراهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the Mu'tazili definition applies to any belief in benefit, regardless of the beneficiary or obstacles.
Correction: The Mu'tazila specify that will is belief in benefit for oneself or a cared-for other, with no impediment. The inclination mentioned by Ash'aris only arises when capability is lacking.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari view presented in this passage, what is will (irada)?
A) Belief in benefit or its estimation
B) A motive (dā'iya) that is identical to belief
C) An inclination (mayl) that follows belief or estimation
D) A repulsion (nufra) following belief in harm

Key Takeaway
The debate on will centers on whether it is merely a cognitive belief or a distinct inclination that follows belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you clearly knew an action was beneficial (e.g., praying, avoiding a sin) yet you felt an internal reluctance. According to this lesson, that reluctance is not simply ignorance—it is a failure of will (mayl). What does that tell you about the condition of your heart? Will you earnestly pray for your inclinations to align with your beliefs starting today?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that belief in benefit for a cared-for other should be enough to move you to action. When you fail to act for the good of someone you love, what does that reveal about your true care? Is your belief merely intellectual, or does it lack the passionate conviction that produces will? Be honest: what must change in your heart?
?
If will is a distinct inclination, then you cannot simply 'will' yourself to do good by force of knowledge alone. How will you cultivate the inner inclination (mayl) toward righteousness—through reflection, dua, or consistent action? What specific step will you take this week to strengthen your will for a good deed you have been neglecting?

8 min
Refining the Mu'tazili Response and the Nature of Inclination
Commentary analyzing the Mu'tazili response on inclination and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentary distinguishes inclination (al-mayl) from belief (i'tiqad) and clarifies the Ash'ari view that irada (will) is a composite of which inclination is a part, not identical to inclination.

Core Idea
Inclination is distinct from belief and not the whole of will; will is a composite with inclination as its final part.

This passage from the commentary on al-Mawaqif delves into the Mu'tazili claim that irada is merely inclination (mayl). The commentator refines this by showing that inclination is posterior to belief, and that irada involves more than inclination. He also addresses the condition that beneficial belief only motivates action when the believer cares for the recipient. By engaging with these nuances, we come to see the Ash'ari position that irada is a structured composite, not a simple inclination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the fragmented style of commentary. Try to follow the thread of the argument despite the density.
The passage is dense and may require re-reading.
2
Identify the main distinction
Focus on how the phrase 'after our belief' indicates posteriority, showing inclination is distinct from belief. Note that this does not prove inclination is not a type of belief in benefit.
Posteriority (ba'diyya) is a key concept here.
3
Examine the condition for motivation
Understand that belief in benefit for another only motivates action if the believer cares for that other. Otherwise, it does not serve as a preponderator (murajjih).
Compare with belief in benefit for oneself, which motivates without extra condition.
4
Grasp the composite nature of irada
The commentator indicates that inclination is neither identical to irada nor a mere part, but rather irada is a set of things with inclination as the final component. This counters the Mu'tazili claim that irada is pure inclination.
The Ash'ari view is more nuanced than simple identification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وهو الميل إلخ ) فإن قلت : قد علمت هذه المغايرة من البعدية المستفادة من قوله نجد من أنفسنا بعد اعتقادنا إلخ . قلت : المستفاد من ذلك كونه مغايراً للاعتقاد المخصوص وأما أنه ليس من جنس اعتقاد النفع، فلا يجوز أن يكون اعتقاد النفع مرتباً على اعتقاد نفع. قوله : ( ومع ذلك لا يريده فقد انفكت الإرادة عن الاعتقاد إلى لقاء حصوله للسياق، فلا تكون هي الاعتقاد وأما أنه الميل فلما مر من أنه إذا حصل حصل الترجيح ( كذا ) . قوله : (ممن يؤثر خيره متعلق بقوله : أو لغيره، فإنّ اعتقاد النفع للغير، وإن كان يمكن حصوله من غير ممانعة ليس مرجحاً لأحد طرفي الفعل ما لم يؤثر المعتقد حينئذ ذلك الغير بخلاف اعتقاد النفع نفسه فإذا أمكن حصوله من غير ممانعة بشيء لا يتوقف الترجح على آخر ويصرف قدرته إليه . قوله : ( وصوله) أي وصول النفع إلى أحدهما أي المعتقد والغير. قوله : ( والميل المذكور إلخ فصاحب الميل المذكور سابق إلى الفعل غير مريد له بعدم تحقق الاعتقاد المذكور من ممانعة نقصان العدة وإن حصل له اعتقاد النفع مطلقاً . الميل لا بالعينية ولا بالجزئية كما سينقله عن الأشاعرة، أو يكون مجموع أمور يكون الميل جزءاً أخيراً منها فلا تكون الإرادة ميلاً فقط كما هو مدعاهم . قوله : (ومنع ذلك لا يريده ) أي لا يقال له المريد . إليهما جميعاً، ومم الاعتقاد، قوله: (بل نقول : هي اعتقاد نفع الخ ضمير له ولغيره للمعتقد المفهوم من ) وضمير وصوله إلى النفع وضمير خيره إلى المعتقد، والغير على سبيل البدل وضمير أحدهما وممن يؤثر في موضع الصفة للاعتقاد أي اعتقاد كائن ممن يؤثر وهو للاحتراز عمن اعتقد النفع ولا يختاره فلا يكون باعثاً له على الفعل . قوله : ( والميل الذي ذكرتموه إنما يحصل الخ فإن قلت : الميل المذكور إن كان إرادة
His saying: 'And it is the inclination, etc.' For if you say: You have known this distinctness from the posteriority derived from his saying 'we find in ourselves after our belief, etc.' I say: What is derived from that is that it is distinct from the specific belief; but as for it not being of the genus of belief in benefit, it is not permissible that belief in benefit be consequent upon belief in benefit. His saying: 'And despite that he does not intend it' – so irada is separated from belief until its occurrence for the context, so it is not belief; and as for it being inclination, it is as previously mentioned that when it occurs, preponderance occurs (thus). His saying: 'from whom he cares for his good' is attached to his saying 'or for another'; for belief in benefit for another, even if it can occur without prevention, is not a preponderator for one of the two sides of the act unless the believer cares for that other at that time, unlike belief in benefit for oneself; for if it can occur without prevention by something, preponderance does not depend on another and he directs his power to it. His saying: 'its arrival' – i.e., the arrival of benefit to one of the two, i.e., the believer and the other. His saying: 'and the mentioned inclination, etc.' – so the possessor of the mentioned inclination is precipitating to the act, not intending it, due to the absence of the mentioned belief because of prevention by lack of equipment, even if he has absolute belief in benefit. The inclination is neither identical nor partial, as he will later transmit from the Ash'ari; or it is a set of things of which inclination is the final part, so irada is not merely inclination as they claim. His saying: 'and preventing that, he does not intend it' – i.e., he is not called 'intender'. To both of them, and from belief. His saying: 'Rather we say: it is belief in benefit, etc.' – the pronoun 'his' and 'for another' refers to the believer understood from the context, and the pronoun 'its arrival' refers to benefit, and the pronoun 'his good' refers to the believer, and 'another' is alternative; the pronoun 'one of them' and 'from whom he cares' is in the position of an adjective for belief, i.e., belief occurring from one who cares, which is a precaution against one who believes in benefit but does not choose it, so it is not a motivator for him to act. His saying: 'and the inclination you mentioned only occurs, etc.' – for if you say: the mentioned inclination, if it is irada...
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a commentary analyzing the Mu'tazili response and the nature of inclination. It begins by discussing the evidence for the distinctness of inclination from belief: the phrase 'after belief' indicates posteriority, but that only shows distinctness from the specific belief, not that inclination is not itself a type of belief in benefit. It then examines the claim that irada is separate from belief because one may believe in benefit yet not intend, which shows irada is not belief. The commentary clarifies that belief in benefit for another only preponderates if the believer cares for that other; otherwise it does not motivate. It discusses the concept of 'arrival of benefit' and argues that inclination, when present, leads to action without relying on another factor. Crucially, the commentary distinguishes between inclination being identical with irada, or being a part of it (not merely partial, and not identical), referencing the Ash'ari position that irada is an inclination but not merely inclination? Actually it says the inclination is neither identical nor partial, but rather a set of things with inclination as the final part, so irada is not merely inclination. Finally, it examines the condition that the believer must care for the other, contrasting this with inclination that occurs even when belief is absent due to impediments. The passage ends mid-sentence, suggesting the commentary continues.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari define irada as pure inclination (mayl).
Correction: The commentary indicates that irada is a composite of multiple elements, of which inclination is the final part; it is not merely inclination.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does the phrase 'after our belief' indicate about the relationship between inclination and belief?
A) Inclination is identical to belief
B) Inclination is posterior to belief and distinct from it
C) Inclination is a part of belief
D) Inclination is the cause of belief

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view refined in this passage is that irada (will) is not simply an inclination, but a composite state in which inclination is the culminating part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a relationship where you knew someone needed your help but you didn't act. Do you truly 'care' for that person as the passage demands, or is your belief in their need merely theoretical? Today, make a concrete plan to act on one such belief – and follow through before the day ends.
?
The passage argues that will is not just a simple inclination but a composite of many factors. Examine your own will to pray regularly: what components are missing that prevent your inclination from reaching completion? Identify one missing factor and take a step to supply it – whether it is knowledge, environment, or sincerity.
?
Consider a habitual sin you intend to stop but still commit. Is your 'belief in benefit' of stopping truly present, or is your will lacking because your inclination is not aligned? Ask yourself: do I really believe this is better for me? If yes, then why does my inclination not follow? Commit to one concrete change in your routine that will align your inclination with your belief.
